Razi's Critique of Shared Necessity
Razi elaborates on why composition implies possibility, addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that composition alone proves possibility, and his defense that necessity is a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Composition implies possibility; necessity is a positive attribute.

This dense passage is Razi's self-critical commentary on the argument that shared necessity leads to composition and thus possibility. He refines the reasoning, showing that one need not go beyond composition to establish contingency, and he defends the positive nature of necessity against objections.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that Razi is responding to his own earlier argument about necessity being shared among multiple beings. He begins by asserting that composition alone suffices to prove possibility.
The passage is part of a larger discussion on divine attributes.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice the statement: 'every composite is possible.' This is the foundational premise Razi relies on.
3
Follow the circle objection and reply
Track the objection that if essence entails necessity, then necessity depends on essence, creating a circular dependency. Razi shows this does not make the essence an effect of another; rather, it shows the essence is necessary only through an attribute.
The argument gets subtle; note the distinction between essence and attribute.
4
Understand the positivity of necessity
Razi counters the claim that necessity is not a positive attribute by arguing that its opposite (non-necessity) is non-existence, so necessity is positively predicated.
This is a key defense of the ontological status of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) أقول إن لزوم التركيب من تقدير كون الوجوب . شتركا بين اثنين كان الواجب أن يقتصر على ذلك لانه قد تبين أن كل مركب ممكن . ثم قوله له بعد ذلك : فإن استلزمت الهوية الوجوب كان الوجوب محلول الغير هذا خلف فيه نظر ، لأن الخلف لو كان الواجب معلول الغبر لولا الوجوب أما إن كانت هويته مستلزمة لوجوبه وكان وجوبه محتاجا إلى هويته لم يلزم منه كون الهوية معلولا للغير، بل يلزم منه كون الهوية غير واجبة بانفرادها إنما تكون واجبة لصفة تقتضيها ذاتها ، ولو قال فى الأول الوجوب صفة فهي غير واجبة بدون الموصوف بها فيكون معلول الغير حصل مقصوده ، والاعتراض عليه يكون الوجوب غير ثبوتى باطل على مذهبه، فإن نقيض اللاوجوب المحمول عليه العدم، فالوجوب يكون محمولا عليه . وقوله : وإن لم يكن الوجوب واجبا كان ممكنا فالواجب لذاته أولى أن يكون ممكنا ، إعادة المضى وقد مره
(2) I say: The necessity of composition from assuming that necessity is shared between two [beings] would require that one stop at that, for it has been shown that every composite is possible. Then his saying after that: 'If the essence entails necessity, then necessity would be dependent on another—this is a [vicious] circle'—this requires consideration, because the circle would require the necessary to be an effect of another if not for necessity. But if his essence entails his necessity and his necessity is in need of his essence, it does not follow that the essence is an effect of another; rather, it follows that the essence is not necessary by itself, but is necessary due to an attribute that its essence necessitates. And if he had said in the first that necessity is an attribute, it would not be necessary without the described, so it would be an effect of another, and his purpose would be attained. The objection that necessity is not positive is false according to his doctrine, for the opposite of non-necessity that is predicated is non-existence, so necessity would be predicated. And his saying: 'If necessity is not necessary, it would be contingent, so the Necessary in Himself is more deserving of being contingent'—this is a repetition of what has passed.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the previous argument. Razi responds to his own earlier argument about composition. He notes that composition alone implies possibility, so one could stop there. He then examines the case where essence entails necessity: if necessity depends on essence, the essence is not necessary by itself but by an attribute, and that attribute's necessity might be derived. He criticizes the objection that necessity is not a positive attribute, saying it is false because its negation is non-existence, so necessity is a positive predication. The final point about contingency of necessity is a repetition of an earlier argument.

Key Terms
تركيب
ممكن
صفة
محمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity cannot be a positive attribute; it is a relation or negation.
Correction: Razi argues that the opposite of non-necessity is non-existence, so necessity is a positive predication.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that necessity is a positive attribute?
A) Because its opposite (non-necessity) is non-existence.
B) Because it is shared between two beings.
C) Because it is composite.
D) Because it is contingent.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that composition alone proves possibility, and necessity is a positive attribute, not a mere negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's necessity is a positive attribute that He alone possesses, and composition implies possibility, then in what area of your life are you subtly associating partners with Him, treating something else as necessary alongside Him? How will you uproot that today?
?
Razi shows that even a logical argument about attributes can refine your understanding of Tawheed. Do you approach your belief in God as something to be intellectually purified, or do you settle for a vague notion? What specific step will you take this week to deepen your understanding of God's attributes?
?
The passage demonstrates that the Necessary Being cannot be composite. Yet you often combine love of God with love of worldly things. How does this truth expose the inconsistency in your heart, and what will you sacrifice to remove that composition from your worship?

The Argument That the Necessary is Necessary from All Aspects
Razi presents a philosophical proof for the necessity of the necessary being from all aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the necessary by essence must be necessary from all aspects, and why this argument depends on the denial that relations are real external existents.

Core Idea
If the necessary depended on anything external, it would be possible, not necessary.

This lesson presents the core argument used by philosophers to establish that the necessary being (God) is necessary from every perspective. The argument shows that any dependence on an external factor would contradict necessary existence. However, the argument only holds if we accept that relations are not real entities outside the mind. Let's examine the reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional proof.
The passage begins with the thesis and then presents a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the chain: if an attribute depends on an external, then the essence depends on that external, leading to possibility. Identify the contradiction.
The chain is: essence -> attribute -> external factor -> dependency -> contradiction.
3
Understand the condition
The argument only works if relations are not existential. Consider why this condition is necessary for the argument to hold.
If relations are real, the dependency might not affect the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته . إذ لو فرضنا اتصافه بأمر ثبوتى أو سلبي لم يكن في تحقيقه ذاته لتوقف حصول ذلك الأمر له أو انتفائه عنه على حضور أمر خارجي أو عدمه. فذاته موقوفة على حضور ذلك الحصول أو الانتفاء، والموقوف على الموقوف على الغير موقوف على حضور ذلك الغير. فالواجب لذاته موقوف على الغير فيكون ممكنا لذاته هذا خلف. وهذه الحجة لا تتمشى إلا بنفى كون الإضافات أمورا وجودية في الأعيان . أقول هذه المسألة هى المعركة بين المتكلمين والفلاسفة، لأنه يقتضى كون الواجب واجبا من جهة الفاعلية فيكون فعله قديما والمتكلمون لا يسلمون هذا. وقوله إذا فرضنا اتصافه بأمر موقوف على أمر خارجي فذاته موقوفة على الغير ليس بصحيح، لأن توقف أمر متعلق بالواجب وغير الواجب لا يوجب توقف الواجب على غير الواجب، بل لا يوجب إلا توقف ذلك الأمر على غير الواجب. والإضافات والسلبيات كلها كذلك وهم يقولون باتصافه بهما. فإذا ليس مرادهم من قولهم الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته هذا، بل المراد أنه واجب من جميع جهات يتعلق به وحده ولا يتوقف على الغير ككونه مصدرا ومبدأ، لا ككون الغير صادرا عنه ومتأخرا منه، فإن بين الاعتبارين فرقا.
Issue: The necessary by essence is necessary from all its aspects. For if we suppose it is described by a positive or negative thing, then its essence would not be sufficient for its realization, because the obtaining of that thing for it or its negation depends on the presence of an external thing or its absence. Thus its essence depends on the presence of that obtaining or negation, and that which depends on that which depends on something else depends on the presence of that something else. So the necessary by essence depends on something else, and thus it would be possible by essence—this is a contradiction. And this argument only works by negating that relations are existential things in external realities. I say: This issue is the battleground between the theologians and the philosophers, because it implies that the necessary is necessary from the aspect of agency, so His act would be eternal, and the theologians do not accept this. And his statement: “If we suppose He is described by something that depends on an external thing, then His essence depends on something else” is not correct, because the dependence of something related to the necessary and other than the necessary does not necessitate the dependence of the necessary on other than the necessary; rather, it only necessitates the dependence of that thing on other than the necessary. Relations and negations are all like that, and they (the philosophers) say He is described by them. So their meaning when they say “the necessary by essence is necessary from all aspects” is not this; rather, it means that He is necessary from all aspects that pertain to Him alone and do not depend on something else, like being source and principle, not like something else proceeding from Him and being posterior to Him, for there is a difference between the two perspectives.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a theological-philosophical discussion about the necessary by essence (wājib li-dhātih) being necessary from all aspects. The author presents a proof: if the necessary were described by any positive or negative attribute that depends on an external factor, then its essence would depend on that external factor, making it possible—a contradiction. However, the author notes that this argument only works if relations (iḍāfāt) are not considered existential realities. Then the author provides his own commentary ('aqūl): this is a point of conflict between theologians (mutakallimūn) and philosophers. Philosophers hold that this implies God's act is eternal, which theologians reject. The author explains that the philosophers' statement does not mean that God's essence depends on external things; rather, His necessity pertains only to aspects that are solely about Him (e.g., being the source and principle), not to relations of creation which are posterior. He clarifies that the dependence of an attribute related to God does not imply God's essence depends on something else.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
جهات
إضافات
سلبيات
ثبوتى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'necessary from all aspects' means God cannot have any attributes at all.
Correction: The argument only targets attributes that depend on external causes, not essential attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude if an attribute depends on an external factor?
A) The necessary remains necessary.
B) The necessary becomes possible.
C) The attribute is necessary.
D) The external factor is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The necessary being cannot depend on any external factor, otherwise it would be possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself anxious about losing something you rely on, does your heart reveal that you consider that thing a necessary support? How does recognizing God's absolute independence from all externals challenge your practical trust in Him today?
?
If you were to truly internalize that the necessary being depends on nothing outside itself, what would you stop fearing, and what would you start depending on exclusively?
?
In what area of your life are you acting as if God's grace depends on your circumstances, and how can you repent of that by reorienting your dependence solely on Him?

Razi's Critique: Clarifying the Philosophers' Intention
Razi critiques the philosophers' argument and clarifies their intended meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theologians reject the philosophers' argument and what the philosophers actually mean by 'necessary from all aspects' according to Razi.

Core Idea
The philosophers do not claim God's acts are eternal; they mean He is necessary in His essence, not in His relations.

After presenting the argument, Razi provides his own commentary. He explains that this issue is a major point of contention between theologians and philosophers. The theologians reject the implication that God's act must be eternal. Razi then critiques the reasoning and clarifies that the philosophers' slogan refers only to aspects that pertain to God alone, not to His relations with creation.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's commentary
Read the Arabic passage from 'أقول' onwards and its translation. Identify the two main parts: the theological conflict and the logical critique.
The passage begins by naming the issue as a battleground.
2
Understand the theological objection
Why do theologians reject the argument? Note that it implies God's act is eternal, which contradicts the Islamic belief in temporal creation.
Theologians hold that God creates in time, so His act cannot be eternal.
3
Grasp the distinction of aspects
Razi clarifies that the philosophers mean necessity only in aspects intrinsic to God (like being source), not in aspects involving creation. Distinguish between the two perspectives.
The philosophers affirm relations and negations but do not see them as affecting God's essential necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته . إذ لو فرضنا اتصافه بأمر ثبوتى أو سلبي لم يكن في تحقيقه ذاته لتوقف حصول ذلك الأمر له أو انتفائه عنه على حضور أمر خارجي أو عدمه. فذاته موقوفة على حضور ذلك الحصول أو الانتفاء، والموقوف على الموقوف على الغير موقوف على حضور ذلك الغير. فالواجب لذاته موقوف على الغير فيكون ممكنا لذاته هذا خلف. وهذه الحجة لا تتمشى إلا بنفى كون الإضافات أمورا وجودية في الأعيان . أقول هذه المسألة هى المعركة بين المتكلمين والفلاسفة، لأنه يقتضى كون الواجب واجبا من جهة الفاعلية فيكون فعله قديما والمتكلمون لا يسلمون هذا. وقوله إذا فرضنا اتصافه بأمر موقوف على أمر خارجي فذاته موقوفة على الغير ليس بصحيح، لأن توقف أمر متعلق بالواجب وغير الواجب لا يوجب توقف الواجب على غير الواجب، بل لا يوجب إلا توقف ذلك الأمر على غير الواجب. والإضافات والسلبيات كلها كذلك وهم يقولون باتصافه بهما. فإذا ليس مرادهم من قولهم الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته هذا، بل المراد أنه واجب من جميع جهات يتعلق به وحده ولا يتوقف على الغير ككونه مصدرا ومبدأ، لا ككون الغير صادرا عنه ومتأخرا منه، فإن بين الاعتبارين فرقا.
Issue: The necessary by essence is necessary from all its aspects. For if we suppose it is described by a positive or negative thing, then its essence would not be sufficient for its realization, because the obtaining of that thing for it or its negation depends on the presence of an external thing or its absence. Thus its essence depends on the presence of that obtaining or negation, and that which depends on that which depends on something else depends on the presence of that something else. So the necessary by essence depends on something else, and thus it would be possible by essence—this is a contradiction. And this argument only works by negating that relations are existential things in external realities. I say: This issue is the battleground between the theologians and the philosophers, because it implies that the necessary is necessary from the aspect of agency, so His act would be eternal, and the theologians do not accept this. And his statement: “If we suppose He is described by something that depends on an external thing, then His essence depends on something else” is not correct, because the dependence of something related to the necessary and other than the necessary does not necessitate the dependence of the necessary on other than the necessary; rather, it only necessitates the dependence of that thing on other than the necessary. Relations and negations are all like that, and they (the philosophers) say He is described by them. So their meaning when they say “the necessary by essence is necessary from all aspects” is not this; rather, it means that He is necessary from all aspects that pertain to Him alone and do not depend on something else, like being source and principle, not like something else proceeding from Him and being posterior to Him, for there is a difference between the two perspectives.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a theological-philosophical discussion about the necessary by essence (wājib li-dhātih) being necessary from all aspects. The author presents a proof: if the necessary were described by any positive or negative attribute that depends on an external factor, then its essence would depend on that external factor, making it possible—a contradiction. However, the author notes that this argument only works if relations (iḍāfāt) are not considered existential realities. Then the author provides his own commentary ('aqūl): this is a point of conflict between theologians (mutakallimūn) and philosophers. Philosophers hold that this implies God's act is eternal, which theologians reject. The author explains that the philosophers' statement does not mean that God's essence depends on external things; rather, His necessity pertains only to aspects that are solely about Him (e.g., being the source and principle), not to relations of creation which are posterior. He clarifies that the dependence of an attribute related to God does not imply God's essence depends on something else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' claim denies that God has any relations with creation.
Correction: Razi clarifies that they affirm relations and negations but consider them not affecting His necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the philosophers' intended meaning when they say 'the necessary by essence is necessary from all aspects'?
A) He is necessary in every respect, including His relations with creation.
B) He is necessary only in aspects that pertain to Him alone, like being the source.
C) He is necessary only in His essence, not in His attributes.
D) He is necessary only in positive attributes, not negations.

Key Takeaway
God's necessity pertains only to His essence and intrinsic aspects; relations with creation do not compromise His necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi distinguishes between aspects intrinsic to God and those involving others. In your life, do you often define yourself by your relations (status, possessions) rather than by your essence before God? What would change if you saw yourself as God sees you—intact and necessary regardless of what proceeds from you? Make a concrete decision this week to detach your self-worth from one relational aspect.
?
The philosophers' mistake was to think that if an attribute depends on external factors, then the essence depends on them. Where in your relationship with God do you fear that your failures or dependencies affect His love or acceptance of you? How does Razi's correction free you to approach God with confidence despite your dependencies? Commit to praying about that fear today.
?
Razi says God is necessary in aspects that pertain to Him alone. What does that mean for your prayer life? Are you treating God as if He needs your worship to be complete, or do you worship Him knowing He is perfectly self-sufficient? Pause and ask: 'O Allah, teach me to worship You for who You are, not for what I need from You.'

Divine Attributes

The Necessary Essence and Its Attributes
Razi discusses and critiques the view that the necessary essence can have attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the necessary by essence can have multiple attributes necessitated by its essence, and the philosophical objection to that view.

Core Idea
A single necessary essence cannot be the source of many attributes without compromising its oneness.

In this passage, Razi presents a key issue in divine attributes: can the necessary being have multiple attributes that are necessitated by its essence? The matn gives a positive view, but the commentary introduces a powerful objection from the philosophers, showing that this position is not widely accepted except among the Ash'arites. We will explore both sides and the underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the matn's claim
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (up to 'لم يبق واحدة') and its translation. Notice the assertion that essential necessity is specific to the identity, and that unity is a portion for that identity.
The matn is the main text before the author's commentary.
2
Understand the philosophical objection
Read the commentary starting from 'أقول' to 'إلا الأشاعرة'. The author rejects the matn's view based on the principle that the one cannot be a source for more than one.
Here 'الحكماء' refers to the philosophers, particularly the Peripatetics.
3
Identify the exception
Notice that the author says only the Ash'arites hold this view. This is an important sectarian marker.
The Ash'arite position on attributes will be explained later.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The author ends with an analogy about knowledge and unity. Consider how this illustrates the conceptual distinction between identity and attribute in the intellect.
This analogy helps clarify the difference between unity as a concept and unity in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الواجب لذاته يجوز أن تعرض له صفات تستلزمها ذاته . فيكون الوجوب الذاتي حصة لتلك الهوية فقط وسائر النعوت واجبة لوجوب تلك الهوية. وتكون الوحدة حصة لذلك الهوية من حيث هى هى، وإن كانت إذا أخذت مع الوحدة لم يبق واحدة . أقول : هذا ممتنع عند الحكماء لأنهم يقولون : الواحد لا يكون من حيث هو واحد مصدراً لأكثر من واحد. وقوله : وسائر النعوت واجبة لوجوب تلك الهوية معناه أن صفاته المتكثرة ممكنة لذواتها والواحد لا يكون إلا الذات، مع أنها مع الوحدة لا تكون أيضاً واحدة ومع الصفات تكون كثيرة. وهذا ليس ما ذهب إليه الحكماء ولا المتكلمون إلا الأشاعرة كما سيجيء شرحه. وقوله : الوحدة حصة لتلك الهوية وإذا أخذت مع الوحدة لم يبقى واحدة، مجرى مجرى قول من يقول إذا علم الإنسان الواحد كان ذلك الواحد مع علمه به اثنين فإن الوحدة هي تعقل العقل لعدم انقسام لتلك الهوية.
Issue: The necessary by essence can have attributes that its essence necessitates. So the essential necessity is a portion for that identity only, and the rest of the attributes are necessary due to the necessity of that identity. And unity is a portion for that identity insofar as it is itself, even if when taken with unity it does not remain one. I say: This is impossible according to the philosophers because they say: The one insofar as it is one cannot be a source for more than one. And his statement: 'the rest of the attributes are necessary due to the necessity of that identity' means that His multiple attributes are possible in themselves, and the one is nothing but the essence, even though they (the attributes) together with unity are not one, and with the attributes they are many. This is not what the philosophers or the theologians hold, except the Ash'arites, as will be explained. And his statement: 'unity is a portion for that identity, and when taken with unity it does not remain one' is similar to the statement of someone who says: When one knows the one human, that one with his knowledge of it becomes two. For unity is the intellect's conceptualization of the indivisibility of that identity.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether the necessary by essence can have multiple attributes that are necessitated by its essence. The main text asserts that essential necessity is specific to the identity (huwiyya) and other attributes are necessary due to that identity's necessity. It also discusses unity (al-waḥda) as a portion for the identity. The author's commentary ('aqūl) rejects this view from the perspective of the philosophers (ḥukamā'), who hold that the one cannot be a source for many. The author explains that the statement that attributes are necessary due to the identity implies they are possible in themselves, which conflicts with the necessary essence being one. He notes that this view is not held by philosophers or most theologians, except the Ash'arites, as will be explained later. The analogy of knowledge and the known illustrates how unity and attributes coexist in intellect but not in external reality.

Key Terms
صفات
وجوب ذاتي
هوية
وحدة
واحد
كثرة
أشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessary essence can have multiple attributes that are distinct from its essence without any problem.
Correction: The philosophers argue that a truly simple and one essence cannot be the source of multiple distinct attributes, as that would compromise its oneness.

Checkpoint
According to the author's commentary, which school of thought holds that the necessary essence can have multiple attributes necessitated by the essence?
A) The philosophers.
B) The Mu'tazilites.
C) The Ash'arites.
D) All theologians.

Key Takeaway
The necessary essence's oneness is incompatible with having multiple attributes that are distinct from the essence, according to the philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak of God as 'Merciful' or 'Just', do you mentally picture these as separate additions to His essence? How does this lesson force you to reconsider the unity of God in your own worship and speech? Will you now watch your words more carefully?
?
The passage compares knowing a single human to making it two in the intellect. Think about your own thoughts: when you think about God's attributes, do you unintentionally divide Him into parts? What specific step will you take today to ensure your understanding of God remains unified?
?
If the philosophers are right that the one cannot be a source for many, then how do you reconcile God's oneness with the diversity of His creation? Does this lesson unsettle you? What question does it raise that you have not yet answered?

Critique of the Philosophers' Argument on Divine Attributes
The commentator refutes the philosophers' argument that 'God existed in pre-eternity' is an affirmative attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun reject the philosophers' argument that 'God existed in pre-eternity' is an affirmative attribute, and how they expose its logical flaws.

Core Idea
The philosophers' argument collapses under logical scrutiny, revealing its own contradiction.

The commentator turns to a specific argument raised by the philosophers concerning the status of divine attributes. They claimed that the statement 'God existed in pre-eternity' must be an affirmative attribute because its opposite would also be affirmative. Here, the commentator dismantles this argument, pointing out logical inconsistencies and reaffirming the mutakallimun's position that the attributes are not originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the commentator's direct and critical tone.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find the statement where the philosophers argue that 'God existed in pre-eternity' is an affirmative attribute because its opposite is also affirmative.
This claim is presented right after the opening lines.
3
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator points out that this argument leads to a contradiction: from 'God existed in pre-eternity' one could derive 'God did not exist in pre-eternity.' He also notes redundancy if existence and non-existence are taken as contradictory propositions.
The referee to prior discussion ('we have mentioned repeatedly') indicates this is a well-known flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول صفات الله تعالى عند من يقول بكونها زائدة على ذاته ليست بمحدثة، ولذلك كان من الصواب أن يقول وهو الله وفاته، وفى المحدث يقول وهو ما عداه وعدا صفاته، والشبهة التي أوردها الفلاسفة اخترعها هو لأجلهم وليست بشيء، فإنه قال كان الله موجوداً فى الأزل صفة ثبوتية لأنه نفيض ما كان كذلك ولو كان النقيض ثبوتيا لمكان المعدوم موصوف بصفة ثبوتية أقول قد مر ما في هذه الطريقة من الغلط وأيضا يقتضى كان الله موجودا فى الأزل ما كان الله موجودا في الأزل، وهو قضية ولا يكون شيء من المعدومات موصوفا هذه الصفة وإن جعل بإزائه شيئا كان معدوما موجودا في الأزل حتى يصير ذلك المعدوم موصوفا بأنه لم يكن في الأزل لم تكن هذه القطية نقيضاً الأولى لتخالف موضوعهما، وإن أراد بذلك أن الكون واللاكون متناقضان والكون محمول على الله واللاكون محمول على العدم فيكون الكون وجودياً، كان إيراد اقضيتين بدل مفردين حشو والكلام على مثل هذه المناقضة وفسادها مما ذكرناه مراراً، وللفلاسفة شبه غير هذه في القدم الزمان سيأتي ذكرها والجواب عنها .
وقوله : قال المتكلمون معنى كون الله قديماً أنا لو قدرنا أزمنة لانهاية لها لكان الله معها كلام لم يرتضيه كل المتكلمين، فإن كون الشىء مع الشيء لا يتحقق إلا فيما كان في الزمان أو تقدير زمان، والمحققون منهم يقولون معناه أنه غير مسبوق بغيره، لا يقال إن السبق أيضاً لا يتحقق إلا بتقدير زمان، لأنهم يقولون سلب السبق منه لا يقتضي كونه زمانيا .
(1) I say: The attributes of Allah according to those who say they are additional to His essence are not originated. Therefore, it would be correct to say 'He is Allah and His attributes', and regarding the originated, he says 'it is other than Him and other than His attributes'. The doubt raised by the philosophers he invented for their sake and it is nothing. For he said: 'God existing in pre-eternity' is an affirmative attribute because it is the opposite of not being so; if the opposite were affirmative, the non-existent could be described by an affirmative attribute. I say: The error in this method has already been mentioned, and it also requires that 'God existed in pre-eternity' implies 'God did not exist in pre-eternity', which is a proposition, and no non-existent thing is described by this attribute. Even if one places against it something that was non-existent existing in pre-eternity so that that non-existent becomes described as not having been in pre-eternity, this proposition would not be the opposite of the first because their subjects differ. And if he meant by that that existence and non-existence are contradictory, and existence is predicated of God and non-existence is predicated of non-existence, then existence is existential, [then] introducing two propositions instead of two terms is redundant, and the discussion of such contradiction and its invalidity we have mentioned repeatedly. And the philosophers have other doubts regarding temporal eternity, which will be mentioned and answered later. And his statement: The mutakallimun said: The meaning of God being eternal is that if we suppose infinite times, God would be with them — a statement not approved by all mutakallimun, for the being of a thing with another is only realized in what is in time or the estimation of time. And the verifiers among them say its meaning is that He is not preceded by another. It cannot be said that precedence also is not realized except by estimation of time, because they say the negation of precedence from Him does not necessitate His being temporal.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an editorial commentary on the previous argument. The commentator begins by addressing the status of attributes: for those who hold attributes are additional to the essence, they are not originated, so the earlier definition of qadim should include them. He then critiques the philosophers' argument, calling it invented and invalid. He points out a logical flaw: the argument that 'God existed in pre-eternity' is an affirmative attribute because its opposite would also be affirmative would lead to a contradiction or redundancy. He states the error has been covered before. Next, he discusses a statement attributed to the mutakallimun about the meaning of qadim: 'if we suppose infinite times, God would be with them.' He notes that not all mutakallimun accept this, because coexistence requires time or its estimation. The verifiers among them define qadim as 'not preceded by another,' and they argue that the negation of precedence does not imply time. This clarifies that the correct meaning of qadim does not rely on time.

Key Terms
صفات
قديم
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that stating 'God existed in pre-eternity' is an affirmative attribute on par with other positive attributes like knowledge or power.
Correction: The commentator shows that the argument for its affirmativeness leads to logical contradictions, thus it cannot be treated as a simple affirmative attribute.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the philosophers' argument that 'God existed in pre-eternity' is an affirmative attribute?
A) He accepts it as valid.
B) He dismisses it as invented and points out logical errors.
C) He says it is the same as the mutakallimun's view.
D) He says it will be discussed later.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument for the attributes as affirmative collapses under its own logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator says the philosophers' argument is 'nothing' and based on a logical error. Is there an area in your own understanding of Allah's attributes where you might be relying on a flawed human argument rather than what is revealed? Will you examine that area today?
?
The passage ends noting that more doubts will be answered later. Do you actively seek out and study refutations of false beliefs about Allah before they settle in your heart, or do you only react when challenged? Make a concrete plan to learn one solid refutation this week.
?
The philosophers thought their argument was strong, but the commentator dismissed it as redundant. Have you ever been convinced by a sophisticated argument that later turned out to be empty? What did that teach you about the importance of grounding your 'aqeedah in revelation rather than speculation?

The True Meaning of Qadim: Not Preceded by Another
The commentator explains and defends the verifiers' definition of qadim as 'not preceded by another'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verifiers among the mutakallimun define qadim as 'not preceded by another' and how they defend this definition from implying temporality.

Core Idea
Qadim means not preceded by another, without requiring time.

The commentator now discusses a statement attributed to some mutakallimun that the meaning of God being eternal (qadim) is that if we suppose infinite times, God would be with them. He notes that not all mutakallimun accept this, because coexistence requires time. Instead, the verifiers define qadim as 'not preceded by another,' and they argue that the negation of precedence does not entail temporality. This lesson clarifies the precise meaning of qadim in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two definitions presented.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the two definitions
First, some mutakallimun define qadim as coexistence with infinite times. Second, the verifiers define it as 'not preceded by another.' Note the critic of the first definition.
The author says the first definition is not accepted by all.
3
Understand the defense of the verifiers' definition
The verifiers anticipate the objection that 'precedence' also requires time. They respond that the negation of precedence does not necessitate being temporal.
This shows that qadim is a negative attribute, not a positive temporal one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
If God's eternity is a negative attribute (not preceded), does that challenge your tendency to imagine or anthropomorphize Him? Will you commit to worshiping Him as He has described Himself, without adding mental images?

The Sunni and Mu'tazila on Divine Eternals
Razi compares Sunni and Mu'tazila views on divine eternals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Sunnis and Mu'tazila effectively agree on the multiplicity of eternal entities despite terminological differences.

Core Idea
Sunnis and Mu'tazila both affirm multiple eternal realities, differing only in wording.

Razi begins by stating the Sunni position: they affirm the 'eternals' (qadimaat), meaning God's essence and attributes. He then notes that the Mu'tazila outwardly deny this but actually affirm the same concept through their doctrine of the five states (ahwal). This lesson explores this terminological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Sunni-Mu'tazila positions
Read the Arabic and translation to understand both sides.
Focus on the term 'qadimaat' and how each sect interprets it.
2
Identify the Sunni affirmation
Note that Sunnis explicitly affirm that God's essence and attributes are eternal.
They are called 'qadimaat' (eternals).
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila position
See how Razi argues that the Mu'tazila effectively affirm the same through their ahwal theory.
The five states are eternal with the essence.
4
Reflect on the terminological difference
Consider why the Mu'tazila deny the term 'qadimaat' for attributes but accept the meaning.
This highlights a deeper agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : أهل السنة رضى الله عنهم أثبتوا القدماء وهى ذات الله سبحانه وتعالى وصفاته
والمعتزلة بالغوافى إنكاره لكنهم قالوا به في المعنى ، لأنهم قالوا الأحوال الخمسة المذكورة ثابتة في الأزل مع الذات ، فعلى هذا الثابت في الأزل أمور كثيرة ، ولا معنى القديم إلا ذلك ، وأما القول بقديم سوى ذات الله تعالى وصفاته فقد اتفق المسلمون على إنكاره ، لكنهم عولوافيه على السمع ، لأن دليل التمانع لا يدل الاعلى نفي قديم قادر ولا حي فلا
(3) أقول أهل السنة لا يعترفون بإثبات القدماء لأن القدماء عبارة عن أشياء متغايرة كل واحد منهاه
Issue: The Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, affirmed the eternals, and they are the essence of Allah, glorified and exalted, and His attributes. The Mu'tazila denied it [?] but they affirmed it in meaning, because they said the five mentioned states are established in pre-eternity with the essence. So on this basis, what is established in pre-eternity are many things, and the meaning of qadim is nothing other than that. As for the statement that there is an eternal other than the essence of Allah and His attributes, the Muslims have agreed on its denial, but they relied in that on revelation, because the argument of mutual hindrance only indicates the denial of an eternal that is powerful or living, so it does not [indicate any other]. (3) I say: The Ahl al-Sunna do not recognize the affirmation of the 'eternals', because the 'eternals' are expressions for distinct things, each one of them...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a issue (مسألة) on the doctrine of qadimaat (eternal entities). Razi states that Ahl al-Sunna affirm the eternals, meaning God's essence and His attributes. The Mu'tazila outwardly deny this, but they effectively affirm it through their doctrine of the five states (أحوال), which are eternal with the essence. Thus, according to Razi, both parties agree that many things are eternal in pre-eternity, though they differ on terminology. However, Razi adds that Muslims unanimously deny any eternal other than God's essence and attributes, and this denial is based on revelation, not reason, because the argument of mutual hindrance (dalil al-tamānu') only proves the impossibility of two gods with power and life, not other eternal entities. The footnote comment (cut off) notes that Ahl al-Sunna do not actually accept 'eternals' as distinct things with multiplicity, because that would imply multiple separate eternal entities; rather, they consider the attributes as not distinct existences.

Key Terms
قدماء
أهل السنة
معتزلة
أحوال
صفات
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sunnis and Mu'tazila completely disagree on whether attributes are eternal.
Correction: Razi shows they both affirm eternal attributes in meaning; the disagreement is only about the term 'qadim'.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila implicitly affirm the eternality of divine attributes?
A) By explicitly calling attributes qadim.
B) Through their doctrine of the five states that are eternal with the essence.
C) By denying states altogether.
D) By claiming that only God's essence is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Sunnis and Mu'tazila both affirm multiple eternal realities (God's essence and attributes/states), differing only in terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you more attached to the precise words you use to describe God, or to the reality those words point to? Could you recognize a fellow believer who uses different terms but affirms the same truth about His eternal attributes?
?
The Mu'tazila denied the term 'eternals' but affirmed its meaning. Is there any belief you hold so tightly to a specific phrasing that you would reject someone who says the same thing in different words? What would that rejection say about your heart?
?
Razi shows that both Sunnis and Mu'tazila affirm that multiple things are eternal alongside God's essence. Does it trouble you to think of many eternal realities? Or does it expand your reverence for God who shares His eternity with His own attributes? How does that affect your worship?

The Basis for Denying Other Eternals
Razi states Muslim consensus on denying other eternals and its revelatory basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Muslims unanimously deny any eternal other than God's essence and attributes, and that this denial is based on revelation, not on the rational proof of mutual hindrance.

Core Idea
Consensus on no other eternals is based on revelation, not reason.

After discussing the Sunni-Mu'tazila terminological debate, Razi clarifies a broader consensus: all Muslims agree that nothing other than God's essence and attributes is eternal. Importantly, this consensus is rooted in revelation (sam'), not in the rational argument of mutual hindrance (dalīl al-tamānu'), which only disproves two powerful and living gods. This lesson examines the scope and basis of this consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on consensus and its basis
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the consensus and the limitation of the rational proof.
Pay attention to the phrase 'عولوا فيه على السمع'.
2
Identify the consensus
Note that all Muslims agree on denying any eternal besides God's essence and attributes.
This includes Sunnis and Mu'tazila.
3
Understand the basis revelation over reason
Razi explains that this consensus is based on revelation, not on the rational proof (dalil al-tamānu').
The proof only disproves two gods with power and life, not other eternal entities.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why revelation is needed to deny other eternal entities, such as the eternity of the world (a separate debate).
This highlights the limits of rational argument in this area.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : أهل السنة رضى الله عنهم أثبتوا القدماء وهى ذات الله سبحانه وتعالى وصفاته
والمعتزلة بالغوافى إنكاره لكنهم قالوا به في المعنى ، لأنهم قالوا الأحوال الخمسة المذكورة ثابتة في الأزل مع الذات ، فعلى هذا الثابت في الأزل أمور كثيرة ، ولا معنى القديم إلا ذلك ، وأما القول بقديم سوى ذات الله تعالى وصفاته فقد اتفق المسلمون على إنكاره ، لكنهم عولوافيه على السمع ، لأن دليل التمانع لا يدل الاعلى نفي قديم قادر ولا حي فلا
(3) أقول أهل السنة لا يعترفون بإثبات القدماء لأن القدماء عبارة عن أشياء متغايرة كل واحد منهاه
Issue: The Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, affirmed the eternals, and they are the essence of Allah, glorified and exalted, and His attributes. The Mu'tazila denied it [?] but they affirmed it in meaning, because they said the five mentioned states are established in pre-eternity with the essence. So on this basis, what is established in pre-eternity are many things, and the meaning of qadim is nothing other than that. (3) I say: The Ahl al-Sunna do not recognize the affirmation of the 'eternals', because the 'eternals' are expressions for distinct things, each one of them...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a issue (مسألة) on the doctrine of qadimaat (eternal entities). Razi states that Ahl al-Sunna affirm the eternals, meaning God's essence and His attributes. The Mu'tazila outwardly deny this, but they effectively affirm it through their doctrine of the five states (أحوال), which are eternal with the essence. The footnote comment (cut off) notes that Ahl al-Sunna do not actually accept 'eternals' as distinct things with multiplicity, because that would imply multiple separate eternal entities; rather, they consider the attributes as not distinct existences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational proof of mutual hindrance (dalil al-tamānu') is sufficient to deny the eternity of anything other than God.
Correction: Razi clarifies that this proof only disproves two powerful/living gods, not other eternal entities, so revelation is needed.

Checkpoint
Why is the Muslim consensus on denying other eternals based on revelation rather than on the argument of mutual hindrance?
A) Because the argument of mutual hindrance is weak.
B) Because the argument only disproves two eternal beings that are powerful and living.
C) Because the argument is not found in revelation.
D) Because the Mu'tazila reject the argument.

Key Takeaway
Muslims unanimously deny any eternal besides God's essence and attributes, relying on revelation, because rational proof alone cannot eliminate all possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Muslims unanimously deny any eternal besides Allah, but they rely on revelation for this. When you reflect on your own life, where do you rely on your own reason instead of the Qur'an and Sunnah to determine what is true about Allah? Are there areas where your mind has convinced you of something that revelation contradicts? What will you do about that today?
?
Razi points out that the rational proof (dalīl al-tamānu') cannot disprove all false objects of worship. Do you ever assume that your intellect alone can sufficiently lead you to the truth about Allah, forgetting that revelation is necessary? Examine your heart: are there beliefs about Allah that you hold simply because they seem logical, rather than because they come from the Book and Sunnah? Commit to one specific belief you will verify with revelation this week.
?
The passage clarifies that even the attributes of Allah are not separate eternals; they are part of His essence. This means everything about Allah is from Him alone. In your worship, do you ever treat some of Allah's attributes as if they were separate from Him, perhaps focusing too much on one attribute while neglecting others? How can you build a more holistic relationship with Allah based on all His revealed attributes?

Eternal Entities (Qudama') in Islamic Theology
Razi contrasts Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila on eternal entities and explains the role of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna position on eternal entities (qudama'), the Mu'tazila's implicit affirmation through the five states, and why revelation is relied upon for denying any eternal besides God.

Core Idea
Only God and His attributes are eternal; all else is created.

In this passage, Razi navigates a nuanced debate: while Ahl al-Sunna explicitly affirm only God and His attributes as eternal, the Mu'tazila arguably affirm multiple eternal entities through their theory of states (ahwal). This raises the question of what 'eternal' means and how to prove the uniqueness of God's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the technical terms and the flow of argument.
The passage has a main text and a footnote introduced by 'أقول:'. Treat the footnote as part of the passage.
2
Compare positions of Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila
Identify what each group affirms regarding eternal entities. Note how the Mu'tazila's five states effectively make many things eternal 'in meaning'.
Razi says Ahl al-Sunna affirm only God's essence and attributes; Mu'tazila deny outwardly but affirm through the states.
3
Understand the role of revelation
Notice that Muslims agree on denying any eternal besides God, but rely on revelation, not rational proof, because the proof of mutual exclusion (dalil al-tamanu') is limited.
The rational proof only works against powerful/living eternal beings, not all eternal entities.
4
Examine Razi's clarification in the footnote
Read Razi's own commentary: he explains that Ahl al-Sunna do not accept the term 'eternal entities' due to their stance on attributes and distinction, while Mu'tazila differentiate between thubut and wujud.
The five states are a view specific to Abu Hashim.
5
Review the proofs for denying eternal entities
Razi lists three types of proof: the argument from possibility (every possible is originated), the limitation of dalil al-tamanu', and revelatory proofs.
This shows the multifaceted approach in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : أهل السنة رضى الله عنهم أثبتوا القدماء وهى ذات الله سبحانه وتعالى وصفاته والمعتزلة بالغوا [؟] في إنكاره لكنهم قالوا به في المعنى ، لأنهم قالوا الأحوال الخمسة المذكورة ثابتة في الأزل مع الذات ، فعلى هذا الثابت في الأزل أمور كثيرة ، ولا معنى القديم إلا ذلك ، وأما القول بقديم سوى ذات الله تعالى وصفاته فقد اتفق المسلمون على إنكاره ، لكنهم عولوا فيه على السمع ، لأن دليل التمانع لا يدل إلا على نفي قديم قادر ولا حي فلا (٣) . (3) أقول : أهل السنة لا يعترفون بإثبات القدماء لأن القدماء عبارة عن أشياء متغايرة كل واحد منها قديم وهم لا يقولون بالتغاير إلا في الذوات ، أما فى الصفات فلا يقولون بالتغاير ، ولا في الصفات مع الذوات على ما ذهب إليه أبو الحسن الأشعرى ، والمعتزلة يفرقون بين الثبوت والوجود ولا يقولون بوجود القدماء ، والأحوال الخمسة هو قول أبى هاشم وحده ، فإنه علل القادرية والحبيبة [؟] بحالة خامسة هي الأهمية [؟] . وللمسلمين أدلة على نفى القدماء منها بيان أن كل ممكن محدث وذلك يدل على حدوث ما سوى الله تعالى وأما بدليل التمانع فلا يمكن نفي قدماء إذ كانوا أحياء عالمين ، ريدين [؟] إلا أنهم غير قادرين لأن امتناع النمائع [؟] إنما يثبت عند أكثر القادرين ؛ وأما الأدلة السمعية فكثيرة .
Issue: Ahl al-Sunna, may God be pleased with them, affirmed the eternal entities (al-qudama') which are the essence of God and His attributes. The Mu'tazila exaggerated in denying it, but they affirmed it in meaning, because they said the five mentioned states are established in pre-eternity along with the essence. So according to this, the things established in pre-eternity are many, and the meaning of 'eternal' is nothing but that. As for the statement that there is an eternal other than the essence of God and His attributes, the Muslims have agreed on its denial, but they relied in it on revelation, because the proof of mutual exclusion (dalil al-tamanu') only indicates the negation of an eternal powerful or living [being] — no (3). (3) I say: Ahl al-Sunna do not acknowledge the affirmation of eternal entities because 'eternal entities' refers to mutually distinct things each of which is eternal, and they do not affirm mutual distinction except in the essences; as for the attributes, they do not affirm mutual distinction, nor between attributes and essences according to what Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held. The Mu'tazila differentiate between subsistence (thubut) and existence (wujud) and do not affirm the existence of eternal entities. The five states is the saying of Abu Hashim alone, for he caused being powerful and being wise [?] by a fifth state which is the 'lamiyya' [?]. And the Muslims have proofs for denying eternal entities, including the demonstration that every possible is originated, and that indicates the origination of everything other than God. As for the proof of mutual exclusion, it cannot negate eternal entities if they are living, knowing, willing, unless they are not powerful, because the impossibility of mutual exclusion is only established in the case of more than one powerful [being]. As for the revelatory proofs, they are many.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of eternal entities (qudama') – things that are eternal besides God. Razi begins by stating the position of Ahl al-Sunna: they affirm only the essence of God and His attributes as eternal. The Mu'tazila outwardly denied this, but in meaning they also affirmed multiple eternal things because they held that the five states (ahwal) are established in pre-eternity along with the essence. So according to them, many things are established in pre-eternity, which effectively gives the meaning of 'eternal' to these states. However, all Muslims agree that there is no eternal besides God's essence and attributes. They rely on revelation for this, because the rational proof of mutual exclusion (dalil al-tamanu') only refutes the existence of two eternal powerful beings, but does not refute eternal beings that are not powerful or living. Then Razi, in the footnote, clarifies that Ahl al-Sunna do not accept the term 'eternal entities' because it implies distinct things each eternal, whereas they do not affirm distinction between attributes and essence nor between the attributes themselves. The Mu'tazila differentiate between subsistence (thubut) and existence (wujud), so they do not say the states 'exist' but are 'subsistent'. The five states are specifically the view of Abu Hashim, who posited a fifth state (al-alamiyya or al-ahammiyya) to cause the attributes of power and wisdom. Razi then lists proofs Muslims use to deny any eternal entities besides God: the argument that every possible is originated (hadith) shows that everything other than God is originated; the proof of mutual exclusion cannot work against non-powerful eternal beings; and there are many revelatory proofs.

Key Terms

الأحوال الخمسة
أهل السنة
المعتزلة
دليل التمانع
الثبوت
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that all Muslims use rational proofs like dalil al-tamanu' to deny any eternal beside God.
Correction: Razi explains that the rational proof only negates powerful/living eternal beings, so Muslims rely on revelation (sam') for the complete denial. The proof from possibility and revelatory texts are also used.

Checkpoint
Why do Muslims rely on revelation (sam') rather than rational proof to deny any eternal besides God's essence and attributes?
A) Because rational proofs are invalid for this issue.
B) Because the proof of mutual exclusion only negates eternal powerful beings, not all eternal entities.
C) Because revelation is the only reliable source in theology.
D) Because the argument from possibility is insufficient.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm only God's essence and attributes as eternal; the Mu'tazila's theory of states effectively affirms multiple eternal entities in meaning, but all Muslims deny any eternal besides God, relying on revelation since rational proofs like dalil al-tamanu' are insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute timelessness or self-sufficiency to anything in your life besides Allah? What created thing have you unintentionally treated as 'eternal' by relying on it without limit?
?
The lesson shows that even Mu'tazila, who outwardly deny eternal entities, effectively affirm them through their theory of states. In what ways do you outwardly deny any partner with Allah but inwardly treat some aspect of your life—like your reputation, wealth, or health—as if it is beyond change or loss?
?
How does the insufficiency of rational proof to deny all eternal entities affect your trust in revelation? Are there areas where you rely solely on your intellect rather than the revealed text to define your relationship with Allah?

Do Eternity and Origination Count as Attributes?
Razi refutes the attribution of qidam and huduth as attributes, with defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument against treating 'eternity' and 'origination' as attributes, and the nuanced counter-arguments that prevent a simplistic rejection.

Core Idea
Eternity and origination cannot be attributes without infinite regress, but the issue is more subtle than it appears.

Razi presents a debate about whether qidam (eternity) and huduth (origination) are attributes of God or things. He begins with a strong refutation, but then immediately complicates the picture by presenting defenses from the proponents. This passage shows how kalam arguments often require careful qualification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the two claims: 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id says qidam is an attribute; the Karramiyya say huduth is an attribute.
The passage has two parts: the main text and a footnote beginning with 'I say'.
2
Identify the main argument
Razi states that if qidam were an attribute, it would itself be eternal, and similarly huduth would be originated, leading to infinite regress. This is a simple reductio ad absurdum.
3
Explore the counter-arguments
In the footnote, Razi offers defenses: for Ibn Sa'id, non-existence is eternal by itself and does not need an attribute; for the Karramiyya, the attribute of origination is not existent, so it cannot be described as originated. Also, attributes are not described by such terms—only essences are.
These defenses show that the initial refutation is not absolutely conclusive.
4
Reflect on the nuance
Consider how kalam often presents an argument and then examines its limitations. This passage warns against oversimplifying theological claims.
The common misunderstanding is that Razi's initial statement is the final word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة زعم عبد الله بن سعيد منا أن القدم صفة ، وزهمت الكرامية أن الحدوث صفة وهما باطلان ) لأن القدم لو كان صفة لكانت قديمة والحدوث لو كان صفة لكانت حادثة و لزم التسلسل (۲) . (2) أقول : لا يلزم على عبد الله بن سعيد شيء ، لأنه يقول كل ماليس القدم داخلا في مفهومه فإذا وصف القدم احتيج إلى صفة زائدة عليه هى القدم، وأما العدم فلا يحتاج لكونه لذاته قديماً ، وللكرامية أن يقولوا : صفة الحدوث ليست بموجودة على مامر فكيف يوصف بالحدوث ، ولهم أن يقولوا السفات لا توصف بالقدم والحدوث، لأن الاتصاف بهما من شأن الذوات
Issue: 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id from us claimed that eternity (qidam) is an attribute, and the Karramiyya claimed that origination (huduth) is an attribute, and both are false – because if eternity were an attribute, it would be eternal, and if origination were an attribute, it would be originated, and infinite regress would follow (2). (2) I say: Nothing follows necessarily against 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id, because he says: everything in which eternity is not included in its concept, when it is described as eternal, it needs an additional attribute which is eternity. As for non-existence ('adam), it does not need it because it is eternal by itself. And the Karramiyya can say: the attribute of origination is not existent according to what has passed, so how can it be described as originated? And they can say: attributes are not described by eternity or origination, because being described by them is the property of essences.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage deals with the claim that 'eternity' (qidam) and 'origination' (huduth) themselves are attributes. 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id (possibly a theologian) claimed that eternity is an attribute, while the Karramiyya claimed that origination is an attribute. Razi states that both claims are false, because if eternity were an attribute, it would itself be eternal, leading to infinite regress. Similarly, if origination were an attribute, it would be originated, also leading to infinite regress. However, in the footnote, Razi provides a defense for 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id, saying that the argument does not necessarily refute him. According to Ibn Sa'id, anything that does not have eternity included in its concept, when described as eternal, needs an additional attribute of eternity. But non-existence (al-'adam) does not need that, because it is eternal by itself (presumably because it has no beginning). For the Karramiyya, they can argue that the attribute of origination is not existent (as previously discussed, attributes may not be existent), so how can it be described as originated? Or they can argue that attributes are not described by eternity or origination, as being described by these belongs to essences (dhawat). So Razi shows that the counter-arguments are not absolutely conclusive.

عبد الله بن سعيد
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because Razi says the claims are false, they are universally rejected.
Correction: Razi himself shows that there are plausible counter-arguments, indicating the nuance in kalam discussions.

Checkpoint

A) Origination is not an attribute because it is not real.
B) If origination were an attribute, it would itself be originated, leading to infinite regress.
C) Attributes cannot be described as originated.
D) The Karramiyya are not reliable.

Key Takeaway
Attributing qidam or huduth as properties requires careful logical consideration; Razi shows both a refutation and the potential counter-arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your understanding of a theological issue is final and that opposing views are simply wrong? This lesson shows that even a seemingly decisive refutation can be challenged. In your own life, are you willing to truly listen to counter-arguments about your beliefs, or do you dismiss them without consideration? Commit to examining one area of your faith where you have been too certain.
?
The Karramiyya argued that attributes are not described by eternity or origination—only essences are. When you speak about God's attributes, do you ever slip into thinking of them as independent entities or as having properties that belong only to God's essence? How can you guard against this subtle form of conceptual shirk in your own reflections?

Refutation of Qadim and Hadith as Attributes
Razi refutes the claim that 'qadim' or 'hadith' are attributes, using the problem of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that 'qadim' and 'hadith' are attributes leads to infinite regress, and how the counter-arguments are addressed.

Core Idea
'Qadim' and 'hadith' cannot be attributes without infinite regress – but the debate is more subtle.

Razi presents a crisp logical argument against two groups who considered 'qadim' or 'hadith' as attributes. However, he then immediately provides their possible defenses, showing the sophistication of kalam. This lesson unpacks the initial refutation, the counter-arguments, and the ultimate resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial claim and argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the first part: if 'qadim' were an attribute, it would need to be qadim itself, leading to regress. Similarly for 'hadith'. This is the core objection.
The argument assumes that attributes themselves must have the same temporal status.
2
Follow the defenses
Razi then adds his own note (أقول) defending Abdullah ibn Sa'id: the attribute 'qadim' is only posited for things that do not already have qadim in their concept, so no regress. For the Karramiyya, he suggests they can argue that the attribute 'hadith' does not exist, so it cannot be described as hadith.
Note the mention of 'non-existence' (al-ma'dum) as a key concept.
3
Understand the referral to prior discussion
Razi concludes by referring to the earlier discussion on the non-existent (ma'dum) as the final answer. This shows that the initial simple refutation is not the last word; deeper principles are at play.
This referral ties back to a fundamental kalam topic about the status of non-existent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة زعم عبد الله بن سعيد منا أن القدم صفة ، وزهمت الكرامية أن الحدوث صفة وهما باطلان لأن القدم لو كان صفة لكانت قديمة والحدوث لو كان صفة لكانت حادثة و لزم التسلسل . أقول: لا يلزم على عبد الله بن سعيد شيء ، لأنه يقول كل ماليس القدم داخلا في مفهومه فإذا وصف القدم احتيج إلى صفة زائدة عليه هى القدم، وأما العدم فلا يحتاج لكونه لذاته قديماً، وللكرامية أن يقولوا : صفة الحدوث ليست بموجودة على مامر فكيف يوصف بالحدوث ، ولهم أن يقولوا السفات لا توصف بالقدم والحدوث، لأن الاتصاف بهما من شأن الذوات . والجواب عنها ما مر في مسألة المعدوم .
A question: Abdullah ibn Sa'id among us claimed that 'qadim' (eternity) is an attribute, and the Karramiyya claimed that 'hadith' (origination) is an attribute, but both are false. Because if 'qadim' were an attribute, it would itself be qadim, and if 'hadith' were an attribute, it would be hadith, and an infinite regress would follow. I say: Nothing follows necessarily against Abdullah ibn Sa'id, because he says that anything for which 'qadim' is not included in its concept, when it is described as qadim, it needs an additional attribute, which is 'qadim'. As for non-existence, it does not need [that] because it is in itself qadim. And the Karramiyya can say: the attribute of origination (hadith) does not exist as previously stated, so how can it be described as hadith? And they can say: attributes are not described as qadim or hadith, because being described with these is a matter of the essences (dhawat). The answer to them is what has already passed in the question of the non-existent (ma'dum).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups: Abdullah ibn Sa'id (an Ash'ari? 'from us' perhaps meaning from the Ash'aris) who considered 'qadim' an attribute, and the Karramiyya who considered 'hadith' an attribute. Razi first gives a brief proof that both views are false: if 'qadim' were an attribute, that attribute would itself be qadim, leading to infinite regress; similarly for 'hadith'. Then he adds an editorial note (أقول) defending Abdullah ibn Sa'id: he only posits the attribute 'qadim' for entities that do not have 'qadim' in their concept, so no regress; as for non-existence, it is intrinsically qadim. For the Karramiyya, Razi says they can argue that the attribute of origination (hadith) does not exist (based on their own principles), so it cannot be described as hadith, and attributes are not described as qadim or hadith in the first place. The final answer, he says, is the same as in the earlier discussion of the non-existent (ma'dum). Thus Razi shows that the initial simple refutation does not necessarily stand, and he provides counter-arguments that defend the two positions, though ultimately he seems to side with the original claim of falsehood via the reference to the previous discussion.

Key Terms

الحدوث
الصفة
التسلسل
المعدوم
الكرامية
عبد الله بن سعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'qadim' and 'hadith' are obvious attributes that can be predicated of God or events without issue.
Correction: The author shows that treating them as attributes leads to logical problems, especially the regress argument, and that the debate requires careful consideration of what can be an attribute.

Checkpoint

A) It would make God composite.
B) It would require an infinite regress because the attribute itself would need to be qadim.
C) It contradicts divine simplicity.
D) It leads to anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether 'qadim' and 'hadith' are attributes hinges on avoiding infinite regress and understanding the nature of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi presents both sides of the argument with nuance, even for views he ultimately rejects. When you hold a theological position, do you honestly engage with the strongest counter-arguments, or do you settle for a simplified version of the truth? How can you practice intellectual humility like Razi without losing conviction?
?
The concept of 'qadim' (eternity) is applied to both God and, according to one view, non-existence. Consider the contrast between the eternal Creator and your own contingent, fleeting existence. Are you living with a deep awareness that every moment is a gift from the One who alone is truly eternal, or do you act as if your time is your own?

Divine Attributes

The Argument Against Relational Attributes
Razi argues that relational attributes are not real because the essence remains unchanged when relations change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relational attributes like 'above-ness' cannot be real accidents subsisting in the essence.

Core Idea
Relational attributes change while the essence remains, so they cannot be real.

Imam Razi begins by presenting a simple but powerful argument: if a body is not 'above' something and later becomes 'above,' the body itself does not change. This shows that the relation of 'above-ness' is not a real attribute added to the body but merely a conceptual relation. In this lesson, we examine this argument and Razi's marginal clarification between intellectual ('aqli) and assumptive (fardi) entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style of kalam argumentation.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two premises: (1) the body as body is not said in relation, (2) a thing can become above after not being above. From these, it concludes that the essence remains unchanged while the relation is not obtained in both states.
Notice how the argument uses the constancy of essence to deny that the relation is a real attribute.
3
Understand the 'aqli vs fardi distinction
Razi's marginal note clarifies that some relations are 'aqli (intellectual) and some are fardi (assumptive). The above-ness of the sky is 'aqli, while below-ness can be assumed. This distinction helps avoid confusion.
Make sure you grasp the difference: 'aqli is what the intellect necessarily cognizes, fardi is what one assumes regardless of possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في نفس الذات، لأن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم غير مقول بالقياس إلى الغير ، ومن حيث إنه فوق مقول بالقياس إلى الغير، ولأن الشيء قد لا يكون فوق ثم يصير فوقا فالذات باقية فى الحالين والفوقية غير حاصلة في الحالين (۱)
•
ثم إن معمراً من قدماء المتكلمين أثبت لقوة هذه الحجة هذه الأغراض النسبية ولم يجد دافعا للتسلسلات المذكورة فالتزمها وأثبت أعراضا لا نهاية لها يقوم كل واحد منها بالآخر . وقال المتكلمون : هذا باطل، لأن كل عدد موجود فله نصف ونصفه أقل من كله ، وكلما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه فنصفه متناه في العدد ، وكل ما نصفه متناه فكله متناه لأنه ضعف المتناهي
قال معمر : لانسلم أن كل عدد فله نصف بل ذلك من خواص العدد المتناهى ، سلمنا لكن لم قلت بأن كل ما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه ، أليس أن مقدورات الله تعالى أقل من معلوماته وتضعيف الألف مرارا لانهاية لها أقل من تضعيف الألفين مرارا لانهاية لها (۲)
ونحن نقول : حجة الفلاسفة على إثبات النسب يقتضى كون المتقدم والمتأخر صفتين موجودتين وذلك محال ، لأن الإضافتين توجدان معا ومحلاهما يوجدان معا فالقبل موجود مع البعد هذا خلف، ولأنا نحكم على اليوم الماضى فى اليوم الحاضر بكونه ماضيا والمفهوم من كونه ماضيا ليس أمرا سلبيا لأنه صار ماضيا بعد ما لم يكن ماضيا فإذا هو ثبوتى ، وليس ثبوته في الذهن فقط ، فإنا لو فرضنا عدم الفرض والاعتبار فذلك اليوم ماض في نفسه وليس عبارة عن نفس ذلك اليوم ، لأنه حين كان حاضرا لم يكن ماضيا ، فيلزم أن يكون وصف كونه ماضيا عرضا حقيقيا قائما به حال عدمه فيكون الموجود قائما بالمعدوم وهو محال (۳)
(۱) أقول : كون الشيء عقليا كفوقية السماء يباين كونه فرضيا، فإن تحتية السماء ربما يفرض ، بل المقلى الذى يجب أن يحدث فى العقل إذا عقل العقل ذلك الشيء كفوقية السماء . وأما الفرضي فهو الذي يفرضه الفارض وإن كان محالا والذهن يشتملهما ، ويجب أن يفهم كل واحد منهما لئلا يقع بسبب الاشتباه غلط (۲) أقول : غير المتناهى لايصير متناهيا بنقصان كل شيء منه ، والتي ربما يكون متناهيا من وجه غير من وجه فلحقه خواص المتناهى من الوجه الأول وخواص غير المتناهى من الوجه الآخر ، وهذا كتضعيف الألف والألفين مرارا لانهاية لها فيكون أحد غير المتناهيين نصفا الآخر ، ولا يلزمه منه تناهى أحدهما. (۳) أقول : قد بينا أن الإضافة تعقل عند تصور المضافين والمتقدم والمتأخر موجودان في التصور معا ولا يلزم ذلك قيام موجود معدوم ، بل يلزم حدوث معقول متصور وذلك غير محال ، وقد عرفت أن ذلك ثابت فى نفس الأمر من غير الفرض وليس بالذهن الصرف.
In the essence itself, because the body as body is not said in relation to another, and as 'above' is said in relation to another, and because a thing may not be above then becomes above, so the essence remains in both states while the above-ness is not attained in both states. (1) Then Muammar, an early mutakallim, affirmed these relative accidents due to the strength of this argument and found no way to avoid the mentioned infinite regresses, so he accepted them and affirmed infinite accidents, each subsisting in another. The mutakallimun said: This is false, because every existing number has a half, and its half is less than the whole, and whatever is less than another is finite, so its half is finite in number, and whatever has a finite half is itself finite because it is double the finite. Muammar said: We do not concede that every number has a half; rather that is a property of finite numbers. Granted, but why did you say that whatever is less than another is finite? Are not Allah's powers less than His knowledge, and the doubling of a thousand an infinite number of times less than the doubling of two thousand an infinite number of times? (2) And we say: The philosophers' proof for affirming relations requires that the prior and the posterior be two existent attributes, and that is impossible, because the two relations exist together and their loci exist together, so the prior would exist with the posterior – this is a contradiction. And because we judge of yesterday in the present day that it is past, and the concept of its being past is not a negative matter because it became past after it was not past, so it is positive. And its positivity is not only in the mind, for if we assume the absence of assumption and consideration, that day is past in itself and is not the same as that day because when it was present it was not past. So it follows that the description of its being past is a real accident subsisting in it at the time of its non-existence, so an existent would subsist in a non-existent, which is impossible. (3) (1) I say: The thing being 'aqli like the above-ness of the sky differs from its being fardi; for the below-ness of the sky may be assumed. Rather the 'aqli is what must occur in the intellect when the intellect cognizes that thing, like the above-ness of the sky. As for the fardi, it is what the assumer assumes even if it is impossible, and the mind encompasses both. Each must be understood to avoid error due to confusion. (2) I say: The infinite does not become finite by subtracting something from it, and it may be finite in one respect and infinite in another, so it has the properties of the finite in the first respect and the properties of the infinite in the other respect. This is like the doubling of a thousand and two thousand an infinite number of times, so one of the two infinites is half of the other, and it does not necessitate the finitude of either. (3) I say: We have shown that relation is conceived when the two relata are conceived, and the prior and posterior exist in conception together, and that does not entail an existent subsisting in a non-existent; rather it entails the occurrence of an intelligible thing conceived, and that is not impossible. And you have known that this is established in the very matter without assumption, and it is not merely mental.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument. First, a proof is given that relational attributes like 'above-ness' are not real existent attributes because the essence remains unchanged while the relation changes. Second, Muammar's objection is presented: he accepted infinite accidents to avoid infinite regress. The mutakallimun refute him by arguing that every finite number has a half, and an infinite series would not have such halves. Muammar counters that not every number has a half, and that smaller infinities exist. Third, the author presents a direct argument against the philosophers' proof for relations, showing that it would require an accident to subsist in a non-existent (like the past day being described as past while it no longer exists), which is impossible. The marginal notes clarify key concepts: the distinction between 'aqli (intellectual) and fardi (assumptive) entities; the nature of the infinite (that one infinite can be half of another without becoming finite); and the defense that relations are only conceived in the mind and do not imply real accidents subsisting in non-existents.

Key Terms
