?
Razi's argument shows that requiring bodily structure for life leads to absurdities. How does this strengthen your conviction that God's life is utterly unique? What doubts still linger, and how can this argument address them?
?
If life can exist without a body, what does that mean for your understanding of the soul's existence after death? Does this lesson give you more assurance about the afterlife? How will you live differently knowing that God's life is self-subsisting?

Editorial Refinement: One Accident in Many Loci
The editor refines Razi's argument about one accident in many loci.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the editor refines Razi's claim about the impossibility of one accident in many loci and how the circularity objection is resolved.

Core Idea
The impossibility of one accident in many loci is a theological position, not a self-evident rational truth.

This editorial note sharpens a key argument in the discussion of divine attributes. The editor points out that Razi's phrasing could be misleading, and offers a more precise formulation. We'll see why the claim is not rationally impossible but only rejected by most theologians, and how a potential circularity problem is avoided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the editor's corrective tone from the start: 'I say: It would be better for him to say...'
The passage has two parts: a correction and a defense against an objection.
2
Identify the two main parts
First, the editor corrects the claim: it is not rationally impossible but invalid according to most theologians. Second, he addresses an objection that his own argument would make conjunction and separation impossible, then shows why circularity is avoided.
3
Reflect on the circularity solution
Understand why the argument could imply impossibility of conjunction/separation, and how the editor resolves it by noting that life in each part depends on being conjoined, so no circularity follows.
This shows the editor's precision in distinguishing theological from rational impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : الأولى أن يقول حلول العرض الواحد في المحال الكثيرة باطل عند أكثر المتكلمين وليس بمحال في بديهة العقل ولا بالنظر اليقيني كما مر ، وأما الثانى فمحال يقال له الذي ذكرته يقتضى إحالة وجود الاجتماع والافتراق وغيرهما ، لأنه لو توقف اتصاف كل جزء بالاجتماع على اتصاف الجزء الأخير له لزم الدور ، ولكن إن قيل ههنا قيام الحياة بكل جزء موقوف على كون ذلك مجامعا لغيره من الاجزاء لا يلزم منه دور .
I say: It would be better for him to say that the indwelling of a single accident in multiple loci is invalid according to most theologians, not impossible in the intuition of the intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql) nor by certain speculation (al-naẓar al-yaqīnī) as has passed. As for the second, it is impossible. It is said to him: what you mentioned necessitates the impossibility of the existence of conjunction (ijtimāʿ) and separation (iftirāq) and others, because if the qualification of each part with conjunction were dependent on the qualification of the last part to it, circularity would follow. But if it is said here that the subsistence of life in each part is dependent on that part being conjoined to other parts, no circularity follows.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This editorial note refines Razi's argument. The editor suggests that Razi should not claim that one accident in many loci is impossible by self-evident reason (badīha) or certain proof, but rather that it is simply rejected by most theologians. Then he addresses the second part: the editor notes that the argument as presented would also entail the impossibility of attributes like conjunction (ijtimāʿ) and separation (iftirāq) because they would involve similar dependence. However, if we say that life in each part is dependent on that part being conjoined to others, circularity is avoided. So the editor offers a more precise formulation.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
النظر اليقيني
الاجتماع
الافتراق
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of one accident in many loci is a universal rational truth.
Correction: The editor clarifies it is a theological position (invalid according to most theologians), not a self-evident rational truth.

Checkpoint
What does the editor suggest about the claim that one accident cannot inhere in many loci?
A) It is absolutely impossible by reason.
B) It is invalid according to most theologians, not by reason alone.
C) It is proven by the Quran.
D) It is possible.

Key Takeaway
The claim that one accident cannot inhere in many loci is not a self-evident rational truth but a theological position; and the potential circularity regarding conjunction is avoided by understanding the dependence of life on conjunction with other parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological claim that conflicts with your rational intuition, do you assume your reason is correct or do you submit to the consensus of scholars? What does this passage teach you about the humility needed in such moments?
?
The editor here corrects a great scholar like Razi. How does that challenge your view of scholarly infallibility? Are you willing to accept corrections to your own deeply held beliefs, even from those you consider less knowledgeable?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you assume is rationally necessary. Could it be that you hold it not because of self-evident reason but because of theological tradition? How would you investigate that, and what would it cost you to change your mind?

Sciences Differ with Their Objects
Razi states that sciences differ when their objects differ, opposing his father's view of uniformity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that sciences are distinct when their objects are distinct, and how the condition-conditioned distinction applies.

Core Idea
Sciences differ because their conditions differ.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi enters into a methodological debate with his father, who held that all sciences are essentially similar. Razi argues otherwise, using the logic of condition and conditioned. This seemingly technical point has far-reaching implications for how we understand knowledge of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the disagreement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi explicitly opposes his shaykh and father. Then identify the two competing claims: sciences are the same vs. sciences are different.
The father's view is tamathul al-`ulum.
2
Examine the condition argument
Razi argues that the condition (shart) is different from the conditioned (mashrut). For belief in the body's eternity, the condition includes knowledge of eternity; for belief in its temporal origination, the condition includes knowledge of temporality. Hence the beliefs differ.
The differing conditions show the sciences differ.
3
Consider the concession
The passage ends with a possible concession: if one says science as such is not different, but differences arise from objects, then sciences are similar in essence but differ by objects. However, Razi's condition argument suggests the difference is not merely accidental.
This nuance is important for the broader debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: العلوم المتعلقة بالمعلومات المتغايرة مختلفة، خلافا لشيخي ووالدي.

(1) أقول: والده يذهب إلى القول بتماثل العلوم وأنها لا تختلف باختلاف متعلقاتها، والمصنف يقول الشرط مخالف المشروط، وأيضا يقول الاعتقادات متضادة مشروطة بشروط مختلفة، فإن اعتقاد قدم الجسم مشروط بالعلم بالجسم وبالقدم، واعتقاد حدوثه مشروط بالعلم بالجسم وبحدوثه. ولو أنه يقول: العلم من حيث هو علم ليس بمختلف في أنها تختلف بسبب متعلقاته فيكون تماثل العلوم لذاتها واختلافها بسبب اختلاف متعلقاتها.
Question: The sciences related to distinct known things are different, contrary to my shaykh and my father.

(1) I say: His father holds the view of the similarity of sciences and that they do not differ due to the difference of their objects, and the author says that the condition is opposite to the conditioned, and also says that beliefs are contradictory conditioned by different conditions. For the belief in the eternity of the body is conditioned by knowledge of the body and of eternity, and the belief in its temporal origination is conditioned by knowledge of the body and of temporal origination. And if he would say: Science inasmuch as it is science is not different in that they differ due to their objects, so sciences are similar in themselves and their difference is due to the difference of their objects.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This is a short question (mas'alah) asserting that sciences (al-`ulum) that pertain to different known things are themselves different. This is stated in opposition to Razi's shaykh and father. The comment explains the father's view: he believes in the similarity (tamathul) of all sciences—sciences do not differ in essence even if their objects differ. The author (Razi) counters by noting that the condition (shart) is different from the conditioned (mashrut). For example, the belief that the body is eternal is conditioned by knowledge of the body and knowledge of eternity; the belief that the body is originated is conditioned by knowledge of the body and knowledge of temporal origination. The conditions differ, so the beliefs differ. The comment suggests that if one says science as such is not diverse but becomes diverse due to its objects, then sciences are similar in essence but differ accidentally. However, the author seems to reject that by emphasizing the condition argument.

Key Terms
تماثل العلوم
اختلاف المتعلقات
شرط
مشروط
اعتقاد
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the father's view implies sciences are completely identical without any distinction.
Correction: Razi shows that sciences are distinct due to different conditions.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to argue that sciences differ?
A) Knowledge of blackness and whiteness.
B) Belief in the eternity of the body vs. its temporal origination.
C) General and detailed knowledge.
D) Necessary and acquired science.

Key Takeaway
Sciences are differentiated by their conditions, not merely by their objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about Allah, what conditions must be present for that belief to be sound? Does this passage challenge you to examine the basis of your own convictions—not just what you believe, but how you came to believe it?
?
The condition of a belief is distinct from the belief itself. In your own life, how often do you confuse the conditions of your faith (e.g., the proofs you've studied) with the faith itself? Are you willing to have your beliefs tested by their conditions?
?
If sciences differ because their objects differ, what does that say about the urgency of learning the true nature of what you worship? Can you truly say your knowledge of Allah is conditioned by accurate understanding, or have you assumed all beliefs about Him are the same?

Power as an Attribute Distinguishing Voluntary Action
Definition of power and Ash'ari proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari claim that power (qudra) is the attribute that distinguishes voluntary from involuntary movement.

Core Idea
Power is not just limb soundness; it is the quality that makes an action voluntary.

This passage introduces a key debate in kalam about the nature of human power. Razi presents the view that the reference of power in us is either to the soundness of limbs or to something inherited from them. He then states the Ash'ari proof: voluntary movement is distinguished from involuntary movement only by this attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the definition of power and the Ash'ari proof.
2
Identify the two views on power
Notice that Razi presents two interpretations: power as soundness of limbs or as something inherited. Which one is disputed?
3
Understand the proof
The Ash'aris argue that voluntary movement is distinguished from involuntary by power. Why is this important?
This sets up the debate for the objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها القدرة والمرجع بها في حقنا إن كان إلى سلامة الأعضاء فهو معقول وإن كان إلى أمر وراثها فقيه النزاع . احتج أصحابنا بأن حركة المختار متميزة عن حركة المرتعش وليس الامتياز إلا بهذه الصفة . فيقال لهم : متى ثبت هذا الامتياز قبل الاتصاف بالفعل أو حال الانصاف . والأول باطل على قولك لأنك لا نثبت القدرة قبل الفعل ، والثانى كذلك لأن المرتعش كما لا يتمكن من ترك الحركة حال وجودها فالمختار لا يتمكن أيضا من تركها حال وجودها لاستحالة أن يكون الشىء معدوما موجودا في زمان واحد ، ويقال أيضا متى ثبت هذا الامتياز حال ما خلق الله تعالى الحركة أو قبلها، والأول باطل لأن حصول الفعل حال ماخلق الله تعالى ضرورى ، والثانى باطل لأن حصولها قبل أن خلقها الله تعالى محال وعلى التقديرين لا يثبت الاختيار
And among them is power, and the reference to it in us — if it is to the soundness of limbs, then it is intelligible; if it is to something inherited from them, then there is dispute in it. Our companions argued that the movement of the voluntary agent is distinguished from the movement of the tremulous one, and the distinction is only by this attribute. So it is said to them: When is this distinction established? Before the acquisition of the act or at the time of acquisition? The first is false according to your statement because you do not affirm power before the act. The second is likewise because the tremulous one cannot abandon the movement while it exists, and the voluntary agent also cannot abandon it while it exists due to the impossibility of a thing being non-existent and existing at one time. It is also said: When is this distinction established? At the moment God creates the movement or before it? The first is false because the occurrence of the act at the moment God creates it is necessary. The second is false because its occurrence before God creates it is impossible. On both estimates, choice is not established.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a proof and counter-argument regarding the nature of power (qudra). It begins by stating that the reference to power in us (humans) is either to the soundness of limbs (a common interpretation) or to something inherited from them, which is disputed. The author's companions (Ash'aris) argue that the movement of a voluntary agent (mukhtar) is distinguished from that of an involuntary tremulous person (murti'ish), and the distinction lies solely in power (qudra). An opponent objects: when is this distinction established? If before the act, it is false because you do not affirm power before the act. If at the time of the act, it is also false because as the tremulous person cannot abandon the movement during its existence, the voluntary agent also cannot abandon it at that time (since a thing cannot be both existent and non-existent simultaneously). Another objection: is the distinction established at the moment God creates the movement or before it? The first is false because the act's occurrence is necessary when God creates it; the second is false because occurrence before creation is impossible. Thus, choice is not proven on either view.

Key Terms
القدرة
المختار
المرتعش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is merely the soundness of limbs or physical ability.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that power is an attribute that makes an action voluntary, not just physical capacity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari proof regarding power?
A) Power is the soundness of limbs.
B) Power distinguishes voluntary from involuntary movement.
C) Power is inherited from limbs.
D) Power is created by God at the moment of action.

Key Takeaway
Power, in the Ash'ari view, is the attribute that makes an action voluntary, not mere physical capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, like praying or giving charity, what internal quality makes that action truly yours and chosen by you? How would your accountability differ if it were involuntary?
?
Consider a time you acted out of habit or compulsion. Was that action truly voluntary? If power is the attribute that distinguishes voluntary action, are you using your power intentionally every day?
?
If power is a gift from Allah that makes your actions meaningful, how often do you thank Him for it? What would change in your life if you remembered that each voluntary act is a sign of His mercy?

Objections to Power as a Distinguishing Attribute
Objections to the Ash'ari proof on power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the Ash'ari position on power and why they conclude that choice (ikhtiyar) is not established.

Core Idea
The distinction between voluntary and involuntary acts cannot be pinned down in time.

Having stated the Ash'ari proof, Razi now presents a powerful objection. The opponent asks: when exactly is the distinction established? Through a series of dilemmas, the argument shows that power cannot serve as a distinguishing attribute, undermining the notion of human choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read carefully the two dilemmas presented against the Ash'ari proof.
2
Analyze the first dilemma
The opponent asks whether the distinction is before the act or at the time. See why both options are rejected.
3
Analyze the second dilemma
Next, the opponent asks whether the distinction is at the moment of divine creation or before. Again, both are false.
4
Draw the conclusion
On both estimates, choice is not established. What does this imply for the Ash'ari view?

Source Text

ومنها القدرة والمرجع بها في حقنا إن كان إلى سلامة الأعضاء فهو معقول وإن كان إلى أمر وراثها فقيه النزاع . احتج أصحابنا بأن حركة المختار متميزة عن حركة المرتعش وليس الامتياز إلا بهذه الصفة . فيقال لهم : متى ثبت هذا الامتياز قبل الاتصاف بالفعل أو حال الانصاف . والأول باطل على قولك لأنك لا نثبت القدرة قبل الفعل ، والثانى كذلك لأن المرتعش كما لا يتمكن من ترك الحركة حال وجودها فالمختار لا يتمكن أيضا من تركها حال وجودها لاستحالة أن يكون الشىء معدوما موجودا في زمان واحد ، ويقال أيضا متى ثبت هذا الامتياز حال ما خلق الله تعالى الحركة أو قبلها، والأول باطل لأن حصول الفعل حال ماخلق الله تعالى ضرورى ، والثانى باطل لأن حصولها قبل أن خلقها الله تعالى محال وعلى التقديرين لا يثبت الاختيار

Proof That Power Is Concurrent with the Action
Razi proves that power must be simultaneous with the act, against the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that power must be simultaneous with the act, and how he responds to the Mu'tazili objections.

Core Idea
Power is an accident that does not endure, so it must coexist with its act.

This passage tackles one of the central debates in Islamic theology: does a person have power before they act, or only at the moment of acting? Razi, following the Ash'ari tradition, argues that power is an accident that cannot endure, so it must be concurrent with the action. The Mu'tazila raise three strong objections, which Razi systematically refutes. Engaging with this argument will sharpen your understanding of causality, divine attributes, and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core proof
Read Razi's initial proof carefully: power is an accident, accidents do not endure, so if power preceded the act, it would be absent when the act occurs. This establishes the simultaneity thesis.
This is the foundation; grasp it before moving to objections.
2
Examine the three Mu'tazili objections
Identify each objection: (1) the case of the unbeliever commanded to faith, (2) that power is only needed to bring the act into existence, and (3) that simultaneity would imply either the eternity of the world or temporal origination of God's power.
These objections seem strong; Razi's answers require careful analysis.
3
Analyze Razi's answers
Study each answer in turn. Notice how the first answer turns the objection back on the Mu'tazila, the second uses analogy, and the third distinguishes divine from human power.
Focus on the logical structure, not just the conclusions.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this argument affects the concepts of obligation (taklif) and divine justice. Why does the Ash'ari position not lead to injustice?
This step ties the passage to larger debates about predestination and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : القدرة مع الفعل خلافا للمعتزلة

لنا أن القدرة عرض فلا تكون باقية ، فلو تقدمت على الفعل لاستحال أن يكون قادرا على الفعل لان حال وجود القدرة ليس إلا عدم الفعل ، والقدم المستمر يستحيل أن يكون مقدورا وحال حصول الفعل لا قدرة

احتجوا بأن الكافر حال كفره مكلف بالإيمان فلو لم يكن قادرا على الإيمان حال كونه كافرا كان ذلك تكليفا بما لا يطاق، ولأن الحاجة إلى القدرة لأجل أن يدخل الفعل من العدم إلى الوجود وحال حدوث الفعل قد صار الفعل موجودا فلا حاجة به إلى القدرة ، و لأنه لو وجب أن تكون القدرة مع المقدور لزم إما قدم العالم أو حدوث قدرة الله تعالى .

والجواب عن الأول : أنه وارد عليكم أيضا لأنه حال حصول القدرة لا يمكنه الفعل وحال حصول الفعل لا قدرة له عليه ، فإن قلت إنه فى الحال مأمور لا بأن يأتي بالفعل في الحال بل بأن يأتى به فى ثانى الحال ، قلت هذا مغالطة ، لأن كونه فاعلا للفعل إما أن يكون نفس صدور الفعل عنه وإما أن يكون أمرا زائدا عليه فلو كان الأول استحال أن يصير فاعلا قبل دخول الفعل فى الوجود ، وإذا كان كذلك استحال أن يقال إنه مأمور بأن يفعل في الحال ، وإن كان الثاني كانت تلك الفاعلية أمرا حادثا فيفتقر إلى الفاعل ، والكلام في كيفية فعلها كالكلام فى الأول فيلزم التسلسل .

وعن الثاني : أنه منقوض بالعلة والمعلول أو الشرط والمشروط . الثالث : أن المؤثر فى وجود أفعال الله تعالى تعلق قدرته بها زمان حدوثها ، وأما التعلقات السابقة وهن فلا أثر لها البتة وهذا لا يمكن تحققه في قدرة العبد لأنها غير باقية
Issue: Power is concurrent with the act, contrary to the Mu'tazila.

Our proof: Power is an accident, so it does not endure. If it preceded the act, then it would be impossible for one to be powerful over the act because at the moment of power's existence there is only the absence of the act, and continuous absence cannot be a thing over which one has power, and at the moment of the act's occurrence there is no power.

They objected: The unbeliever while in his unbelief is obligated to faith, so if he were not capable of faith while being an unbeliever, that would be an obligation of what cannot be borne. Also, because the need for power is for the act to come from non-existence into existence, and at the moment of the act's occurrence the act has come into existence, so there is no need for power. And also, if power had to be concurrent with the thing over which it has power, it would necessitate either the eternity of the world or the temporal origination of God's power.

The answer to the first: It applies to you as well, because at the moment of power's existence he cannot perform the act, and at the moment of the act's occurrence he has no power over it. If you say: He is commanded not to perform the act now but to perform it in the next moment, I say: This is a sophistry. For his being a doer of the act is either the same as the act's issuing from him or something additional to it. If the first, it is impossible for him to become a doer before the act enters into existence, and if that is so, it is impossible to say he is commanded to do the act now. If the second, then that agency is an accident that requires a doer, and the discussion about how it acts is like the first, leading to infinite regress.

As for the second: It is rebutted by the example of cause and effect, or condition and conditioned. The third: The effective factor in the existence of God's acts is the attachment of His power to them at the time of their occurrence; the previous attachments have no effect whatsoever. This cannot be realized in the power of the servant because it is not enduring.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a substantive theological argument (mas'ala) establishing that power (qudra) must be simultaneous with the act (al-qudra ma'a al-fi'l), contrary to the Mu'tazila who held that power precedes the act. The proof: Power is an accident ('arad) and accidents do not endure (baqa'). If power preceded the act, then at the moment power exists, the act does not exist, and continuous non-existence cannot be the object of power. At the moment the act occurs, power would have ceased (since it does not endure), so there would be no power at the time of the act. Thus power must be concurrent. The Mu'tazila raise three objections. First: The unbeliever is obliged to faith while still an unbeliever, which requires power to be present before the act. The answer: This objection applies equally to them, because at the moment of power (if it preceded) the act is not yet possible, and at the moment of the act there is no power. If they claim the command is for a future moment, that reasoning leads to an infinite regress. Second: The need for power is only to bring the act from non-existence to existence, so once the act exists, power is superfluous. The answer: This is refuted by the analogy of cause and effect or condition and conditioned, where the cause/condition must be present at the effect/conditioned. Third: If power had to be with the act, then either God's acts would be eternal (since His power is eternal) or His power would be temporal. The answer: For God, His power attaches to the act at the moment of its occurrence; prior attachments have no effect. This cannot apply to human power because it is non-enduring.

Key Terms
القدرة
الفعل
المعتزلة
العرض
الباقي
التكليف
العلة والمعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power must precede the act because commands are given before actions, and one needs power to comply.
Correction: Power is an accident that does not endure, so if it preceded the act, it would vanish before the act occurs. The Ash'ari position holds that power is created at the moment of the act, not before.

Checkpoint
What is the core reason given by Razi that power cannot precede the act?
A) Power is an accident and accidents do not endure.
B) The act is impossible without power.
C) God's power is eternal.
D) The Mu'tazila are mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Power must be concurrent with the act because it is a non-enduring accident; otherwise, one would have power when there is no act and no power when the act occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay a good deed because you feel you are not 'ready' or 'strong enough' today, are you implicitly assuming that power precedes the act? How does knowing that God gives you the power exactly at the moment of the act challenge your procrastination? What will you do differently right now?
?
Have you ever made excuses for a sin by saying, 'I just couldn't resist'? Reflect on the fact that at the moment of the sin, God gave you the power to choose otherwise. Does your excuse deny that concurrent power? What does this lesson demand from you the next time you face temptation?
?
If you truly believe that power is only at the moment of the act, how should that change the way you pray? Do you rely on your own prior resolve or on the power Allah creates at the moment of each prostration? Commit to one concrete change in your prayer based on this truth.

The First Objection: Commanding a Disbeliever to Believe
Razi refutes the first Mu'tazili objection on power timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that commanding a disbeliever to believe is an impossible command if he lacks power at the moment of command.

Core Idea
The objection applies equally to the Mu'tazila themselves.

The Mu'tazila argued that a disbeliever commanded to believe right now cannot have power only at some later moment – that would be commanding the impossible. Razi begins by stating the Ash'ari position that power exists only at the time of the act, then turns the objection back, showing the Mu'tazila face the same dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari premise
Read the opening lines where Razi explains that power exists only when the act is occurring, and the eternal past cannot be an object of power.
This sets up the doctrine that power is simultaneous with the act.
2
Identify the first objection
Find the Mu'tazili claim: a disbeliever commanded to believe must have power at the time of command, otherwise it's an impossible obligation.
3
Follow Razi's counter-argument
Razi argues that if power must be present before the act, then the Mu'tazila have the same problem since their own view also lacks power before the act. He then examines the nature of agency to block the Mu'tazili escape using a delayed command.
This refutation uses a tu quoque (you too) argument and an analysis of agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لان حال وجود القدرة ليس إلا عدم الفعل ، والقدم المستمر يستحيل أن يكون مقدورا وحال حصول الفعل لا قدرة احتجوا بأن الكافر حال كفره مكلف بالإيمان فلو لم يكن قادرا على الإيمان حال كونه كافرا كان ذلك تكليفا بما لا يطاق، ولأن الحاجة إلى القدرة لأجل أن يدخل الفعل من العدم إلى الوجود وحال حدوث الفعل قد صار الفعل موجودا فلا حاجة به إلى القدرة ، و لأنه لو وجب أن تكون القدرة مع المقدور لزم إما قدم العالم أو حدوث قدرة الله تعالى . والجواب عن الأول : أنه وارد عليكم أيضا لأنه حال حصول القدرة لا يمكنه الفعل وحال حصول الفعل لا قدرة له عليه ، فإن قلت إنه في الحال مأمور لا بأن يأتي بالفعل في الحال بل بأن يأتي به في ثاني الحال ، قلت هذا مغالطة ، لأن كونه فاعلا للفعل إما أن يكون نفس صدور الفعل عنه وإما أن يكون أمرا زائدا عليه فلو كان الأول استحال أن يصير فاعلا قبل دخول الفعل في الوجود ، وإذا كان كذلك استحال أن يقال إنه مأمور بأن يفعل في الحال ، وإن كان الثاني كانت تلك الفاعلية أمرا حادثا فيفتقر إلى الفاعل ، والكلام في كيفية فعلها كالكلام في الأول فيلزم التسلسل . وعن الثاني : أنه منقوض بالعلة والمعلول أو الشرط والمشروط . وعن الثالث : أن المؤثر في وجود أفعال الله تعالى تعلق قدرته بها زمان حدوثها ، وأما التعلقات السابقة وهن فلا أثر لها البتة وهذا لا يمكن تحققه في قدرة العبد لأنها غير باقية
Because the state of existence of power is nothing but the absence of action, and the eternal past cannot be an object of power, and the state of occurrence of the act has no power. They objected: that the disbeliever in his state of disbelief is commanded to believe, so if he were not capable of belief while being a disbeliever, that would be an impossible command; and because the need for power is only to bring the act from non-existence to existence, and at the moment of occurrence of the act the act has come into existence, so there is no need for it; and because if it were necessary that power accompany the object of power, it would entail either the eternity of the world or the temporal origination of God's power. The response to the first: that it applies to you as well, because at the moment of obtaining power he cannot perform the act, and at the moment of the act he has no power over it. If you say that he is commanded not to do the act immediately but to do it in the next moment, I say this is a sophistry, because his being an agent of the act is either the very emanation of the act from him or an additional matter. If the first, it is impossible for him to become an agent before the act enters into existence. If that is so, it is impossible to say that he is commanded to do the act immediately. If the second, then that agency is a temporal accident and requires an agent, and the discussion about how it occurs is like the discussion about the first, leading to infinite regress. The response to the second: that it is refuted by the cause and effect or the condition and conditioned. The response to the third: that the effect in the existence of God's acts is the attachment of His power to them at the time of their occurrence, and the prior attachments have no effect at all, and this cannot be realized in the power of the servant because it is not lasting.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an objection-response exchange regarding the nature of power. It begins with the Ash'ari position that power exists only at the moment of the act. Then three Mu'tazili objections are raised: (1) a disbeliever is commanded to believe now, but if he lacks power, it is an impossible command; (2) power is needed to bring the act from non-existence to existence, but once the act exists, power is unnecessary; (3) if power must accompany its object, it leads to problems of eternity or temporality. Razi responds: to the first, he turns the objection back against the Mu'tazila, showing they face the same problem; to the second, he says it is refuted by analogy to cause and effect; to the third, he distinguishes God's power from human power, emphasizing that God's power is effective only at the time of creation, not beforehand, and human power is non-lasting.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
تكليف (obligation)
فاعلية (agency)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if power is only at the moment of the act, then commanding before that moment is unjust.
Correction: Razi shows this is not an Ash'ari problem alone; the Mu'tazila also lack power before the act, and the agency argument prevents even delayed commands.

Checkpoint
What is the main strategy in Razi's refutation of the first objection?
A) He denies the disbeliever is commanded.
B) He turns the objection back against the Mu'tazila and analyzes agency.
C) He says God does not command the impossible.
D) He argues power is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the first objection by showing it equally applies to the Mu'tazila and by analyzing agency to preclude a delayed command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah, do you immediately assume you have the power to obey, or do you often procrastinate, thinking you'll do it later when you 'feel' more capable? What does Razi's argument reveal about the danger of delaying obedience?
?
If you ever excused yourself from an obligation by saying 'Allah didn't give me the ability right now,' how does Razi's refutation of the Mu'tazila challenge that excuse? Are you truly powerless, or is your excuse a subtle form of self-deception?
?
Razi shows that agency is inseparable from the act. In your daily life, do you separate your intention from your action, believing that just 'meaning well' is enough? What does this lesson demand you change about how you connect your will to your deeds?

The Second Objection: Need for Power to Bring Act into Existence
Razi refutes the second Mu'tazili objection on power timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that power is only needed to bring the act from non-existence to existence, and thus power need not coincide with the act.

Core Idea
The objection is refuted by analogy to cause and effect.

The Mu'tazila argued that since power is only needed to bring the act into existence, once the act exists, power is no longer required. This would mean power exists before the act, not simultaneously. Razi refutes this by pointing out the analogous relationship between cause and effect or condition and conditioned.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Look at the Mu'tazili claim: power is needed only to cause transition from non-existence to existence; after the act exists, power is superfluous.
This implies power temporally precedes the act.
2
Understand Razi's analogical refutation
Razi simply says this is refuted by the relationship between cause and effect or condition and conditioned: just as a cause must coexist with its effect, power must coexist with the act.
This is a concise refutation; it appeals to common logical principles.
3
Reflect on the simplicity of the response
Notice that Razi does not elaborate much – he finds the analogy sufficient to show the Mu'tazili reasoning fails.
The refutation relies on the accepted principle that the cause must be simultaneous with its effect.

Source Text

What one step can you take today that acknowledges His power is simultaneous with your action?
?
If cause and effect are inseparable, then no act of yours is independent of God's power. How does this reshape your understanding of personal responsibility? In a moment of sin, do you blame yourself or feel that God's power was absent? How can you align your mindset with the truth that His power is always present, yet you are still accountable?

The Third Objection: Implications of Power Accompanying Its Object
Razi refutes the third Mu'tazili objection on power-attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that if power must accompany its object, then either the world is eternal or God's power is temporal.

Core Idea
God's power is effective only at the moment of creation, unlike human power.

The Mu'tazila argued that if power must be with its object, then for God's power to create the world, either the world must be eternal (if power is eternal) or God's power must be temporal (if the world is temporal). Razi distinguishes divine power from human power: God's power attaches to the act only at the time of occurrence, and prior attachments have no effect, a situation not possible for human power since it is non-lasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third objection
The Mu'tazila claim: if power must accompany the object of power, then for God, either the world is eternal (if power is eternal) or God's power is temporal (if the world is temporal).
This is a dilemma aimed at the Ash'ari view.
2
Understand Razi's distinction
Razi distinguishes between divine and human power. For God, power attaches to the act only at the moment of its occurrence; prior attachments have no effect. This avoids the dilemma.
Human power is non-lasting, so it cannot have prior attachments.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Razi's response relies on the unique nature of God's power: it is timeless and effective at the precise moment of creation, not requiring continuous attachment.
This resolves the dilemma without compromising divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the third objection by distinguishing divine power, which becomes effective only at the moment of creation, from human power which cannot have ineffective prior attachments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's power is effective only at the moment of His will, how does that challenge your demand for immediate answers or relief? Are you willing to wait on His perfect timing without anxiety?
?
If human power is transient and only effective when present, what does that reveal about your own attempts to control outcomes? Where in your life do you need to surrender control and trust in God's eternal power that creates exactly when He ordains?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed God's power must be continuously effective like human power. Are there areas where you implicitly expect God to act on your schedule or according to human logic? How can you realign your expectations with the truth that His power is not bound by human limitations?

Power is Not Suitable for Opposites
Razi proves that power cannot bring about both opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam Razi, divine power cannot bring about both opposites simultaneously.

Core Idea
Power's relation to opposites must be unequal or require a preponderant, so it cannot produce both.

Imam Razi presents a concise proof against the Mu'tazilite view that power can bring about opposites. He argues that if power had equal relation to opposites, it would need an external preponderant to act, meaning the effect is not solely from power. If the relation is unequal, power only acts on the preponderant side. Thus, power is not suitable for opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then two scenarios.
The passage is dense—take your time.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Look for the two cases: equal relation (sawiyya) and unequal relation. See how Razi handles each.
3
Follow the reasoning for each scenario
For the equal case, understand why a preponderant is needed. For the unequal case, understand why power is only for one side.
The conclusion is that power cannot bring about both opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : القدرة لا تصلح للضدين خلافا للمعتزلة لنا أن القدرة عبارة عن التمكن والمفهوم من ذلك ولأن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين إن كانت على السوية استحال أن تصير مصدرا للأثر إلا عند مرجح فلا يكون مصدر الأثر إلا المجموع فلا يكون الذي فرضناه قدرة مصدر الأثر، فلا يكون قدرة، وإن لم تكن على السوية لم تكن القدرة قدرة إلا على الراجح
Issue: Power is not suitable for opposites, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For us: Power is ability, and the concept from that. And because the relation of power to the two sides, if it is equal, it is impossible for it to become a source of effect except with a preponderant, so the source of the effect would be only the composite, so that which we considered to be power would not be the source of the effect, so it would not be power. And if it is not equal, then power would not be power except for the preponderant.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a proof that power (qudra) is not suitable for bringing about two opposites. Razi argues that if power had equal relation to both opposites, it could only produce an effect with a preponderant factor, meaning the effect would come from the composite, not from power alone, so it would not be power. If the relation is not equal, then power is only effective for the preponderant side, not for both. Thus power cannot bring about both opposites.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
ضدان (opposites)
مرجح (preponderant)
سوية (equality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think power can bring about either of two opposites by itself.
Correction: Razi shows that power alone cannot produce an opposite without a preponderant if the relation is equal, and if the relation is unequal, it only works for the stronger side. Thus power cannot bring about both.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why can power not bring about both opposites?
A) Because power is a single attribute and opposites require different attributes.
B) Because if the relation is equal, power needs a preponderant to produce an effect, so the effect comes from the composite, not from power alone.
C) Because opposites cannot exist together.
D) Because power is only for the preponderant side.

Key Takeaway
Power is not suitable for opposites because either it requires a preponderant or it is limited to one side.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi insists that true power cannot be equally related to two opposites without a preponderant. In your own life, when you feel equally capable of two actions—one pleasing to Allah and one not—what is the preponderant that makes you choose? Is your will enough, or do you rely on Allah's guidance? How does this awareness change your next decision?
?
If God's power requires no preponderant to act (since He is the ultimate source), yet you see two opposite outcomes as equally possible, does your trust in His power and wisdom truly tip the balance in your heart? When you face uncertainty, do you seek a preponderant from Him, or do you rely on your own reasoning? What concrete step will you take today to submit to His preponderant decree?
?
Razi defines power as ability, but says equal-relation 'power' is not power. Look at your own abilities: are you using them as a neutral tool equally available for good and evil? Or have you committed them to a single purpose for Allah? If you were to die now, would your power have been spent on the preponderant side? Write down one specific ability you will dedicate solely to obedience today.

Will and Aversion: Razi's Distinction
Razi defines will and distinguishes it from desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of will as a distinct attribute from knowledge and desire.

Core Idea
Will is not knowledge of benefit; it is an inclination that follows knowledge.

Razi begins by listing will and aversion among the attributes. He then refutes a common view that reduces will to knowledge, belief, or opinion of benefit. Using introspection and a concrete example, he demonstrates that will is a distinct inclination. This lesson unpacks his argument and clarifies the difference between will and desire.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how Razi introduces will and aversion together.
2
Identify the rejected views
Razi lists three things that some people claim will is: knowledge, belief, or opinion of benefit. Understand each one.
3
Understand the internal argument
Razi argues that we find within ourselves an inclination that is ordered upon knowledge, so they must be different. Think about the relationship between knowledge and inclination.
This is a key proof that will is distinct.
4
Analyse the medicine example
Razi distinguishes will from desire (shahwa) by noting that one's nature recoils from medicine but one still wills it. This shows will can oppose natural desire.
The example clarifies the difference between will and desire.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding divine attributes. If will were just knowledge, it would not be a separate attribute.
This connects to broader theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الإرادة والكراهة ، ومن الناس من زعم أن الإرادة عبارة عن علم الحى أو اعتقاده أو ظنه بأن له فيه منفعة وهو باطل ، لانا نجد من أنفسنا ميلا مرتبا على هذا العلم فيتغايران ، والفرق بين الإرادة والشهوة أن الإنسان ينفر طبعه عن شرب الدواء ثم يريده
And among them are will and aversion. Some people claim that will is the living being's knowledge, or belief, or opinion that there is benefit in it. This is false, because we find within ourselves an inclination ordered upon this knowledge, so they are different. And the difference between will and desire is that man's nature recoils from drinking medicine, then he wills it.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines will (irāda) and distinguishes it from knowledge and desire. Razi refutes the view that will is merely knowledge of benefit, arguing that we experience an inclination that follows knowledge, so they are distinct. He also distinguishes will from shahwa (desire) by the example of medicine: one may have a natural aversion to it but still will to drink it.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
كراهة (aversion)
شهوة (desire)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is simply knowledge, belief, or opinion that something is beneficial.
Correction: Razi argues that will is a separate inclination that arises from such knowledge, not the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
How does Razi distinguish will from desire?
A) Will is always rational, desire is not.
B) Will can override natural aversion, as in drinking medicine.
C) Will is for good, desire is for evil.
D) Will is a type of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Will is a distinct attribute from knowledge and desire; it is an inclination that can exist even when nature recoils.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you knew something was beneficial (like studying, exercising, or speaking the truth) but your natural inclinations recoiled from it. What did you actually do? Was your will strong enough to overcome your desire? If not, why? What would it take for you to develop a will that reliably follows what you know is right, even when it is difficult?
?
When you feel aversion to a religious obligation (like waking for Fajr or giving charity), do you attribute that feeling to a lack of will or to a lack of desire? How does Razi's distinction between will and desire change the way you view these moments? Are you excusing yourself by saying 'I don't feel like it' when the real question is whether you will it or not?
?
Reflect on a moment when you acted out of pure desire, not will. Did it lead to regret? Now contrast it with a moment when you willed something despite aversion. Which felt more like true freedom? Based on this lesson, what is the difference between being a slave to your desires and being a person of will? Which one do you want to be, and what is one step you can take today to strengthen your will?

The Proof That Power Does Not Extend to Opposites
Imam Razi presents the Ash'ari proof that power requires a preponderating factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument Imam Razi uses to prove that power (qudra) cannot be suitable for both opposites.

Core Idea
Power equally related to opposites needs an external preponderator, so it is not the sole cause.

Imam Razi begins by stating the Ash'ari position: power does not extend to both opposites, contrary to the Mu'tazila. He then presents a logical proof based on the relation of power to its two possible effects. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
The passage opens with the issue and a brief reason. Read it carefully.
Notice the term 'mumkina' (possibility) is used.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Imam Razi presents two possibilities: equal relation or unequal. Analyze each.
If equal, a preponderating factor (murajjih) is needed, so power is not the sole source.
3
Connect to the conclusion
See how each possibility leads to the conclusion that power cannot be suitable for both opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : القدرة لا تصلح للضدين خلافا للمعتزلة ) لنا أن القدرة عبارة عن الممكنة والمفهوم الممكنة من ذلك ولأن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين إن كانت علي السوية استحال أن تصير مصدرا للأثر إلا عند مرجح فلا يكون مصدر الأثر إلا المجموع فلا يكون الذي فرضناه قدرة مصدر الأثر ، فلا يكون قدرة ، وإن لم تسكن على السوية لم تكن القدرة ندرة إلا على الراجح (1) من (1) أقول : المعنى لا يختلف بتبديل لفظ القدرة بلفظ المكن ، ومفهوم المكن من هذا ومفهوم التمسكن ذاك يشتركان في مفهوم واحد ، وإنما يختلفان من حيث تعلتهما دارة بهذا وتارة بذاك ، فإن كان المراد من القدرة ذلك الأمر المشترك كانت صالحة للضدين ، وإن كان المراد منها مجموع المشترك سابه الاختلاف لم يقع اسم القدرة على أنواعها إلا بالاشتراك اللفظى ويقع على أنواع تعدد المقدورات وهذا لم يقل به أحد مع وقوله : إن كانت نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السوية احتاجت إلى مرجح وقبل المرجح لا يكون قدرة على الفعل ، يقتضى أن تصير القدرة مبدأ للفعل مع زائد ، وهو عين مذهب من يقول القدرة صالحة للضدين وإنما ذهب من ذهب إلى أن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين لقوله القدرة عرض لا يبقى زمانين ، فالقدرة التي تكون مع أحد الضدين غير التي تكون مع الضد الآخر لا سيما إنهم لا يفرقون بين القدر وبين مبدأ الفعل والترك . ل : إن كانت القدرة عبارة عن سلامة الأعضاء فالعجز عبارة عن آفة تعرض للأعضاء ويكون حينئذ وجوديا ، والقدرة أولى بأن لا تكون وجودية ، لأن السلامة عدم الآفة إن كان العجز ما يعرض للمرتعش وتمتاز به حركة المرتعش عن حركة المختار فالعجز وجودى ، ولعل الأصحاب ذهبوا إليه ، أما إن كانت القدرة هيئة تعرض عند سلامة الأعضاء يعبر عنها بالتمكن أو بما هو علة له فالعجز عدم تلك الهيئة فالقدرة وجودية والعجز عدمى .
Issue: Power (al-qudra) is not suitable for the two opposites, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For us: that power is an expression of possibility (al-mumkina) and the concept of possibility derives from that. And because the relation of power to the two sides, if it were equal, it would be impossible for it to become a source of effect except with a preponderating factor (murajjih). So the source of the effect would only be the composite, so what we assumed to be power would not be a source of effect, so it would not be power. And if it is not equal, then power is not [sufficient] except for the preponderant [side]. (1) From (1) I say: The meaning does not differ by substituting the word 'power' (qudra) with the word 'ability' (makan). The concept of ability from this and the concept of 'tamakkan' (being able) from that share one concept. They only differ in terms of their attachment (ta'alluq), sometimes to this and sometimes to that. If what is meant by power is that common thing, then it is suitable for the two opposites. If what is meant is the composite of the common plus the difference, then the name 'power' does not apply to its kinds except by verbal homonymy (ishterak lafzi) and it would apply to kinds of multiple objects of power, and no one has said this. And his statement: 'If the relation of power to the two sides is equal, it needs a preponderating factor, and before the preponderating factor there is no power to the act', implies that power becomes a principle of the act with an addition, and this is exactly the doctrine of those who say power is suitable for the two opposites. Indeed, those who hold that power is not suitable for the two opposites say so because power is an accident that does not last for two moments, so the power that accompanies one opposite is not the one that accompanies the other opposite, especially since they do not distinguish between power (al-qudra) and the principle of action and inaction. L: If power is an expression of soundness of organs, then inability ('ajz) is an expression of an affliction that occurs to the organs, and then it is existent. Power is more deserving of not being existent, because soundness is the absence of affliction. If inability is what occurs to the tremulous person and by which the movement of the tremulous person is distinguished from the movement of the voluntary agent, then inability is existent. Perhaps the [Ash'ari] followers held this. But if power is a disposition that occurs upon soundness of organs, expressed as capability (tamakkun) or what is a cause for it, then inability is the absence of that disposition. So power is existent and inability is non-existent.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the Ash'ari position that power (qudra) does not extend to both opposites. The author begins by stating the issue: power is not suitable for both opposites, contrary to the Mu'tazila. He then presents a logical argument: if power were equally related to both sides, it would need an external preponderating factor to produce an effect, so the effect would come from the composite, not from power alone; thus the assumed power would not be true power. If the relation is not equal, then power is only for the preponderant side. The commentary (أقول) clarifies that the term 'qudra' may be understood as a common concept (ability), which would then be suitable for opposites, or as a specific instance, which would require verbal homonymy and lead to multiple powers, which is untenable. It further analyzes the opponent's argument: if equal relation implies need for a preponderating factor, then power would need an addition to produce an effect, which is the very position of those who say power is suitable for opposites. The correct Ash'ari position rests on power being an accident that does not last, so the power with one opposite is different from that with the other. The final part (L:) discusses whether inability is positive or negative, concluding that if power is a positive attribute (soundness or disposition), then inability is its absence (non-existent).

Key Terms
القدرة
الضدين
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power being equally related to both opposites means it can produce either at will.
Correction: The argument shows that equal relation actually makes power dependent on an external factor, so power alone is not sufficient; it is not truly suitable for both.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's argument, if power's relation to the two opposites is equal, what is required for an effect to occur?
A) Power alone
B) A preponderating factor
C) The opposite itself
D) The absence of power

Key Takeaway
Power cannot be equally related to both opposites because it would then need an external preponderator, negating its role as the sole source of effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your power to choose good or evil is equally related, and you need God's preponderating factor to act, what does that reveal about your constant dependency on Him? Will you today humble yourself and ask Him to guide your every decision?
?
Consider a sin you repeatedly commit: do you rely on your own strength to abandon it, or do you recognize that your power alone is insufficient? What specific step will you take to seek God's murajjih (preponderating help) to overcome it?
?
If the Mu'tazila claim that your power can produce opposites on its own, that would mean you are independent of God's will in your actions. How does this lesson force you to rethink your sense of self-sufficiency? Will you now repent of any pride in your own abilities and attribute every good deed to God's grace?

Clarifying the Concept of Power and the Nature of Inability
Imam Razi clarifies the meaning of power and discusses the ontology of inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle distinction between power as a common concept and as a specific attribute, and the debate over whether inability is a positive or negative entity.

Core Idea
Power as a common concept is suitable for opposites; power as a specific instance requires verbal homonymy.

After presenting the main argument, Imam Razi includes a commentary that clarifies potential confusion about the term 'power'. He also discusses the nature of inability ('ajz) and whether it is a positive attribute or mere absence. This lesson unpacks these important clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on power and ability
Understand the distinction between qudra and makan, and what it means for power to be a common concept.
2
Analyze the rebuttal to the opponent
See how the opponent's argument actually supports the Ash'ari position if followed consistently.
3
Explore the discussion on inability
Understand the different views on whether 'ajz is existent or non-existent.

?

?

Inability: Positive Attribute or Absence of Power?
Imam Razi reports and evaluates the view that inability is a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether inability is a positive attribute and the methodological principle that claims require evidence.

Core Idea
Without evidence, inability is not proven to be a positive attribute.

In this short passage, Imam Razi examines a specific theological dispute among early Ash'ari scholars: whether the attribute of inability (ajz) is an actual positive quality or merely the absence of power. He judges the positive view as weak due to lack of evidence and then rebuts a counter-argument that tries to settle the matter definitively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is very concise and contains an issue, an evaluation, and a rebuttal.
2
Identify the two views
Identify the view that inability is a positive attribute and the author's criticism of it. Then find the counter-claim that inability is definitely not the absence of power.
The author calls the positive view 'weak' due to lack of evidence.
3
Reflect on the methodological principle
Consider why the author says both interpretations are possible in the absence of evidence. This illustrates a key principle: without decisive proof, multiple possibilities remain open.
This principle applies broadly in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : عند بعض الأصحاب العجز صفة وجودية ) وهو ضعيف لعدم الدليل والذى يقال ليس جمل العجز عبارة عن عدم القدرة أولى من العكس ضعيف ، لانا نساعد على أن كليهما محتمل وأنه لولا الدليل لبقى ذلك الاحتمال (٢)
Issue: According to some of the [Ash'ari] companions, inability ('ajz) is a positive attribute. It is weak due to the lack of evidence. And what is said, that the total of inability is not an expression of the absence of power, is no more preferable than the opposite by weakness, for we agree that both are possible and that were it not for evidence that possibility would remain. (2)
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a short debate on whether inability ('ajz) is a positive attribute or simply the absence of power. Some Ash'ari scholars hold that it is a positive quality. The author judges this view as weak because there is no proof for it. He then criticizes the counter-claim that inability is definitely not the absence of power, stating that both interpretations are equally possible; without evidence, the possibility remains open. The lesson is brief but highlights the methodological principle that in the absence of decisive proof, a claim cannot be preferred over its alternative.

Key Terms
العجز (al-ajz)
صفة وجودية (sifa wujudiyya)
عدم القدرة (adam al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position on attributes is monolithic; in fact, some companions differed on inability.
Correction: Imam Razi reports that only 'some' companions held the positive view, and he criticizes it, showing diversity of opinion.

Checkpoint
What is Imam Razi's main criticism of the view that inability is a positive attribute?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It lacks evidence to support it.
C) It is the position of the Mu'tazila.
D) It leads to a contradiction with God's power.

Key Takeaway
Without decisive evidence, one cannot prefer inability as a positive attribute over it being merely the absence of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about Allah's attributes, do you demand evidence for it, or do you accept it because a scholar said so? How does this passage challenge your dependence on authority over evidence?
?
Consider an area in your life where you are certain about something without proof. Will you now examine that certainty and accept that it might be merely possible, not proven? What would it cost you to hold that possibility open?
?
The passage says that without evidence, both possibilities remain. Are you willing to live with unanswered theological questions, or do you rush to close them? What fear drives you to prefer one possibility over another without proof?

Definition of Will and Refutation of Knowledge-Based Theories
Razi defines will and distinguishes it from knowledge and appetite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi defines will (irada) as distinct from knowledge and how it differs from mere appetite.

Core Idea
Will is a distinct inner inclination, not just knowledge of benefit.

Imam Razi begins his discussion of the divine attribute of will by clarifying what will is not. Some had claimed that will is simply knowledge or belief that an action is beneficial. But as Razi shows, our own experience reveals a separate desire—one that can even oppose our natural inclinations. This passage establishes the conceptual foundation for understanding God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the Arabic word 'الشهرة' likely means 'الشهوة' (appetite) but is a common OCR error.
2
Identify the refuted view
Find the claim that will is knowledge, belief, or opinion of benefit. Underline it in your mind. Then note how Razi rejects it.
3
Notice the argument from self-awareness
Razi says 'we find in ourselves a desire (mayl) consequent to this knowledge, so they are distinct.' This is an appeal to direct inner experience. Ask yourself: do you experience a desire that is separate from your knowledge?
This is a key epistemological move in kalam.
4
Reflect on the medicine example
The example of drinking medicine despite natural aversion shows that will is not the same as appetite. It can overcome natural inclinations. Consider how this applies to divine will.
This sets the stage for understanding God's will as independent of any external compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الإرادة والكراهة ، ومن الناس من زعم أن الإرادة عبارة عن علم الحى أو اعتقاده أو ظنه بأن له فيه منفعة وهو باطل ، لانا نجد من أنفسنا ميلا مرتبا على هذا العلم فيتغايران ، والفرق بين الإرادة والشهرة أن الإنسان ينفر طبعه عن شرب الدواء ثم يريده (٣) لأن العلة أيضا قبل وقوع المعاول ممتنعة العملية وكذلك حال وقوعه وذلك الانتصاف القبلى والحال إليها و الانمول بأن تعلق قدرة الله تعالى زمان حدوث الفعل مؤثر فى وجود الفعل أيضا ليس بشيء لأن الفعل يحب زمان حدوثه وإن لم تكن قدرة ، ومنشأ جميع هذه الأغلاط شيء واحد وهو ما مر ذكره . (٣) أقول : القائل بهذا لا يقتصر على هذا بل يزيد فيه بقوله بأن له أو لغيره ممن يؤثر غره فيه منفعة
And among them is will (al-irada) and aversion (al-karaha). Some people claim that will is the living being's knowledge, or belief, or opinion that there is a benefit in it for him, but this is false, for we find in ourselves a desire (mayl) consequent to this knowledge, so they are distinct. The difference between will and appetite (al-shahwa) is that a person's nature recoils from drinking medicine, then he wills it (3). Because the cause also before the occurrence of effects is prevented from acting, and likewise the state of its occurrence, and that [is?] the prior description and the state to it and the [?] that the attachment of God's power to the time of the occurrence of the act is effective in the existence of the act also is nothing, because the act requires its time of occurrence even if there is no power. The source of all these errors is one thing, which was mentioned before. (3) I say: The one who says this does not limit himself to this but adds to it by saying that there is a benefit for him or for someone else whose good he cares about.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines will (irada) and distinguishes it from knowledge. The author refutes the view that will is merely knowledge, belief, or opinion about benefit, because we experience a desire (mayl) that follows such knowledge but is separate from it. For example, a person naturally dislikes medicine but may still will to drink it, showing will is not the same as natural appetite. The later part discusses philosophical errors about causality and God's power, but the text is corrupt. The commentary (أقول) adds that proponents of the knowledge-based view expand it to include benefit for others.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
الكراهة (al-karaha)
الميل (al-mayl)
الشهوة (al-shahwa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is the same as knowledge of benefit.
Correction: Will is a separate inner inclination (mayl) that follows knowledge; the two are distinct, as we experience them differently.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi reject the view that will is merely knowledge or belief of benefit?
A) Because knowledge is eternal while will is temporal.
B) Because we find a distinct desire (mayl) that follows knowledge.
C) Because the Mu'tazila hold that view.
D) Because will is always about actions, not knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Will is a distinct attribute from knowledge, defined as an inclination that may even oppose natural appetite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when your natural desire recoiled from something but you willed to do it anyway for a higher purpose. Did you recognize that will as separate from your feelings? How might this truth change how you understand your own intentions?
?
If will is not merely knowledge, then knowing what is good is not enough. What does this demand of you in terms of actually inclining your heart towards obedience?
?
When you feel aversion to an act of worship, do you excuse yourself because your 'nature' opposes it? This passage says will can override nature. What will you do the next time your appetite resists?

Resolution and Love in Kalam
Imam Razi defines resolution and love, including their sub-meanings and Ash'ari's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of resolution ('azm) and love (mahabbah) as used in Islamic theology, including their different applications to God and humans.

Core Idea
Resolution and love both relate to will, but their meanings shift between divine and human contexts.

Imam Razi begins by defining two key terms: resolution and love. Both are connected to the concept of will (irada), but their precise meanings depend on who is acting—God or the servant. This lesson unpacks these definitions and clarifies the ambiguity (ishtirak) in the term 'love'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining resolution and love
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a definition of resolution, then moves to love.
2
Understand resolution as decisive will after hesitation
Focus on the definition of 'azm and the explanation of hesitation. Note that resolution occurs when one motive becomes preponderant over others.
The hesitation arises from conflicting intellectual opinions, desires, and repulsions.
3
Identify the homonymous meanings of love
The passage explains that love (mahabbah) is used in multiple senses: as a will that is the principle of action, and as conceptual appreciation of perfection. Find examples for each.
The text gives examples like the love of a parent for a child and the gnostics' love of God.
4
Examine Ash'ari's definition of love
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari defines love from God as the will to honor and reward believers, and from the servant as leaving objection. Compare this with the earlier definitions.
This shows the systematic reduction of divine actions to will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العزم عبارة عن إرادة جازمة ) بعد التردد فيه ، والمحبة عبارة عن الإرادة لكنها من الله تعالى فى حق العبد إرادة الثواب ، ومن العبد فى حق الله تعالى إرادة الطاعة والرضا ، قيل إنه الإرادة وقيل إنه ترك الاعتراض (۲) (۲) أقول : التردد المذكور يحصل من الدواعي المختلفة المنبعثة عن الآراء العقلية وعن الشهوات والنفرات المتخالفة ، فإن لم يوجد ترجيح لطرف حصل التحير وإن وجد حصل العزم، والمحبة تقع باشتراك الاسم على إرادة هو مبدأ فعل وهو الذى نسبه إلى إرادة الثواب أو الطاعة وعلى تصور كمال من لذة أو منفعة أو مشاكلة كحبة العاشق لمعشوقه والمنعم عليه المنعمه والوالد لولده و أما محبة الله سبحانه وتعالى عند العارفين فهو لتصور الكمال المطلق فيه والرضاء . قال أبو الحسن الأشعرى : إنه إرادة إكرام المؤمنين ومثوبتهم على التأييد وهذا من الله تعالى ، وأما من العبد فهو ترك الاعتراض، والرحمة قيل هى النعمة وقال أبو الحسن هي إرادة الإنعام، والولاية إرادة الإكرام والتوفيق ، والبغض والعداوة إرادة الإهانة ، والطرد والتعذيب والسخط إرادة التعذيب، والاختيار عند أبى الحسن هو الإرادة واختار له أى فعل به خيراً ، والمشيئة هى الإرادة ، والكرامية يفرقون بينهما .
Issue: Resolution ('azm) is an expression of a decisive will (irada jazima) after hesitation in it. Love (mahabbah) is an expression of will, but from God Almighty with respect to the servant it is will of reward, and from the servant with respect to God Almighty it is will of obedience and good pleasure. It is said that it is will, and it is said that it is leaving objection (tark al-i'tirad). (2) I say: The aforementioned hesitation occurs from different motives (dawa'i) emanating from intellectual opinions, desires (shahawat) and divergent repulsions. If there is no preponderance for a side, bewilderment occurs; if there is, resolution occurs. Love (mahabbah) occurs by homonymy (ishtirak al-ism) on will that is a principle of action, which is what he attributed to will of reward or obedience, and on conceiving a perfection of pleasure, benefit, or similarity (mushakala) like the love of the lover for his beloved, the benefactor for the one he has favored, and the parent for his child. As for love of God Almighty according to the gnostics ('arifin), it is for conceiving absolute perfection in Him and good pleasure (rida). Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: It is the will to honor the believers and reward them forever, and this is from God. As for from the servant, it is leaving objection (tark al-i'tirad). Mercy (rahma) is said to be blessing (ni'ma), and Abu al-Hasan said it is the will to bestow favor (irada al-in'am). Guardianship (wilaya) is the will to honor and grant success. Hatred (bughd) and enmity ('adawa) are the will to humiliate. Expulsion (tard), punishment (ta'dhib) and displeasure (sakhat) are the will to torment. Choice (ikhtiyar) according to Abu al-Hasan is will, and 'He chose for him' means He did a good thing for him. The will (mashi'a) is the will (irada), and the Karamiyya distinguish between them.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines several key terms. Resolution ('azm) is a decisive will after hesitation, which arises from conflicting motives. Love (mahabbah) is ambiguously applied: it can mean a will that is the principle of action (as in God's will of reward for believers and the servant's will of obedience), or it can mean a conceptual appreciation of perfection (as in human love based on pleasure, benefit, or similarity). For gnostics, love of God is appreciation of His absolute perfection. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari provides systematic definitions: love from God is will to honor and reward believers; from the servant it is non-objection. Similarly, mercy is will to bestow favor; guardianship is will to honor; hatred and enmity are will to humiliate; punishment-related terms are will to torment; choice is will; will (mashi'a) is synonymous with irada, though the Karamiyya differentiate them.

Key Terms
العزم (al-azm)
المحبة (al-mahabbah)
الإرادة (al-irada)
التردد (al-taraddud)
الاعتراض (al-i'tirad)
الرضا (al-rida)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Love (mahabbah) has a single univocal meaning.
Correction: Love is used homonymously with at least two distinct meanings: as a will that is a principle of action, and as conceptual appreciation of perfection. The term applies differently to God and creatures.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, when does resolution occur?
A) After hesitation with preponderance of one motive
B) Without any hesitation
C) Only when there is bewilderment
D) After consultation with others

Key Takeaway
Resolution is decisive will after overcoming hesitation, while love is a homonymous term with different meanings for God and humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hesitation in obeying a command of Allah, what conflicting motives do you find within yourself? Which side ultimately becomes preponderant? How can you strengthen the resolve to choose obedience even when desires pull the other way?
?
The passage distinguishes between human love based on benefit or similarity and the gnostics' love for Allah's absolute perfection. Which type of love do you actually feel towards Allah? Are you content with seeking only pleasure or benefit, or have you tasted love for His perfection? What practical step can you take today to move from self-interested love to love of Allah for who He is?
?
Ash'ari defined love from the servant as 'leaving objection' (tark al-i'tirad). Is there any area of your life where you object to Allah's decree? What would it take for you to truly leave objection and embrace total submission—not just in words but in your heart?

Divine Terms: Mercy, Guardianship, and More
Imam Razi lists additional divine terms and gives Ash'ari's definitions, all as types of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of additional divine terms such as mercy, guardianship, hatred, and choice according to Islamic theologians, especially Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Ash'ari systematically defines all divine actions as expressions of will, reducing apparent attributes to variations of irada.

After defining love, Imam Razi continues with a series of other terms: mercy, guardianship, hatred, punishment, and choice. Each is defined by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari as a specific type of will (irada). This lesson clarifies these definitions and notes the Karamiyya's exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of definitions for each term
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: each term is defined as a specific kind of will.
The terms are presented in rapid succession.
2
Notice the Ash'ari reduction to will
For each term, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari defines it as irada (will) with a specific object: mercy is will to bestow favor, guardianship is will to honor, etc.
This shows a consistent theological method.
3
Understand the consistency
All divine actions and attributes are reducible to God's will. Hatred, enmity, and punishment are also wills—to humiliate or torment.
This avoids positing multiple eternal attributes.
4
Note the Karamiyya's distinction
The passage ends by noting that the Karamiyya distinguish between mashi'a (will) and irada (will), unlike the mainstream view.
This is a minority position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience hardship or calamity, do you recognize it as part of God's will to humiliate or test? How does that reshape your response to pain? Commit to saying 'I accept Your will' the next time you face difficulty.
?
If every divine term like mercy and hatred is simply a variation of God's will, then nothing happens outside His will. Is there any area of your life where you still believe your successes or failures are beyond God's will? Will you trace every outcome back to His will today?
?
The Ash'ari definition of 'choice' (ikhtiyar) is simply will. Reflect on a major life decision you made. Can you honestly say you chose it only because God willed it for you? Will you now surrender your future choices to His will, trusting that He chooses what is good for you?
